After I have completed to you, O sons, this book, which is the last of science in medicine with its supplement, and having finished it with its expositions and declarations, it has seemed right to me that I complete it for you with this treatise, which is a part of surgery, namely, with the hand.
Since operation with the hand is laid down in our region and in our time exclusively: until perhaps knowledge of it has perished, and its trail has been lost: and there remain of it, only a few descriptions in ancient books, which hands have changed: and error and uncertainty occurred to them, until their intentions were closed, and his intention was prolonged. It seemed right to me that I should clarify it with this arrangement of this treatise according to two things, that is, by exposition and declaration, and abbreviation: and that I bring it closer with the forms of tools Cautery, and other instruments of work: for it is necessary to add a declaration, and to distinguish it, because there remains.
The reason why there is no good craftsman of this hand in our time: for the art of medicine is long, and it is necessary that the author of it should exercise himself before it in the science of anatomy, which Galen narrated, until he knows the functions of the limbs, and their forms, and their connection, and separation, and has knowledge of the bones, and nerves, and ligaments, and the number of them, and their course, and their pulsations, and quiet, and places of exit:
And because of that Hippocrates said, since a name is much with a name, and little with an operation, especially in the art of the hand.
We have already said about it at the beginning of this book.
Since he who is not knowing that we have said about anatomy will fall into error, to which human beings are subject: as I saw many of them form about this science, and boast themselves of it without knowledge, and without experiment.
That is because I saw a doctor cut an aposteme scar on the neck of a woman, and he struck some arteries in her neck. Why did the blood flow from the woman's body until she died between his hands?
And I saw another doctor who assumed that he would extract a large stone from the bladder of a man who had been suffering for a long time, and the stone was big. So he approached him and removed it with a twig. Why did the man die on the third day?
And I had already been called to extract it; and I saw from the great size of the stone and the care of the nurse that he would die.
And I saw another doctor who was taking a fee from the Duke of our land for medicine; and there happened to be a black eunuch near him, who broke his thigh bone near the heel: and the doctor continued in his ignorance, and he bound the fracture tightly with strong threads, and did not leave the wound's discharge undischarged.
Then he discharged him according to his desires. Then he discharged him after a few days, and he ordered that the ligatures not be removed: and so he stood until his leg and foot were withered away, and came to destruction.
So I was called up to him; and I set out to loosen the ligatures; and the result was calmness, and he was free of his pains.
However, the corruption had already become established in the member; and I could not restrain it.
Then the corruption stopped walking in the member until it perished.
And I saw another doctor who pierced an aposteme cancerosa; and it was ulcered after a few days until the maliciousness of it increased with his lord.
That is because when it is cancer pure from melancholic humor, then one should not approach it at all with iron unless it sits in a member that can bear it so that the whole of it may be cut off.
And for this reason, my sons, you need to know that surgery is divided into two parts: one in which health is associated with the operation and another in which there is danger in many dispositions.
And I have already aroused it in all places of this book, where the operation comes, in which there is fear and dread.
Why do you need to be afraid of that, and to diminish it: so that the blind may not get accustomed to speaking and being dishonorable.
Therefore take a measure with care and protection, and bring the sick among you with ease and firmness, and use a better way that leads to grace by the mercy of God for salvation and a worthy succession: and remove the terrible diseases from among you, which make you afraid, so that hesitation may come upon you in your faith and your confidence.
For there remains more for our glory, and higher in the world, and lastly to the quantities of us.
For Galenus has already said in some of his advice. Do not cure a disease with medicine: do not call yourself a doctor.

But before I speak about the qualities of the cautery and the contraindications, it is necessary to describe the types of skin lesions that are suitable for it.
I therefore say that the speech about the quality of cautery and its contraindications is a long, subtle, scientific, and secret speech.
A multitude of wise men also spoke in it, and they disagreed with each other; but for me, a little of their speech is enough, fearing extension.
I say that cautery is suitable for all types of skin, whether with or without substance; excepting two types of skin, which are warm and dry without substance, and dry and cold without substance.
But in the case of warm and dry skin with substance, they disagreed. Some said that cautery is suitable for such skin. But others said the opposite, namely that cautery is not suitable for an illness that is both hot and dry, because fire's nature is hot, and it does not suit to heal a hot and dry disease with hot and dry means.
And he who says the opposite said this: "But with cautery, an illness that is hot and dry can be cured in any part of the body, because when you compare the human body to the fire's moisture, you will find that the human body is colder.
And I myself say through my own experience: because through experiment I have often found this to be true.
But it is not necessary to add anything to that, unless one has already tested and exercised in the exercise of the last chapter on cautery, and has recognized the diversity of human skin types, and the nature of illnesses within them, and the causes of those illnesses, and the accidents associated with them, and the time period of their occurrence.
Do not fear either to use the ointments left over: and above all when you are dealing with patients who are cold and wet. For all doctors have come together on them: and they have not disagreed in the treatment with the use of ointment.
And know, sons, that the secret of cure by cautery with fire, and its goodness above cautery with medicine burned together, is because fire is simple, whose action does not reach, unless to the limb that is cauterized: and it does not hurt another member joined with it, except with little harm.
But the action of medicine that is burned comes to that which is prolonged from limbs: and perhaps it causes a difficult cure to heal, and perhaps it kills.
But fire, because of its slightness and goodness of its substance, does not do that, unless it runs short.
But this has already become known to us by experimentation, because of the length of use and study with art, and stability on the truth of things: and for this reason I excuse myself from the length of speech.
And if it is not allowed, because it is not fitting for my book, I would take away from you the secret hidden thing of fire, and the quality of its action in bodies by its expulsion, which expels diseases with philosophical speech that is demonstrated by the minds of your scholars, which has been acquired.
And know, sons, that they disagree in time when cauterization is to be made, and set a better time for the wound.
And I say that cauterization is to be made at all times: because the harm that happens by time is hidden in the treatment, which is drawn with cautery. And especially if cauterization is because of the pains from necessary pains, firm, festivals, which do not tolerate tardiness: because they are afraid of them, that evil may happen, which is greater than a little harm that does not pass by time.
And let not the son of that woman fall in the minds of you all, if he does not take care, unless the people and those who are dull-witted, namely that it is not always a return to health for him when his illness which is cured by cauterization: and they put it down as a consequence, and it is not like what they think; therefore because cauterization is not, except in the place of medicine, which changes the complexion and dries up the humidities that are the causes, why these pains occur.
But cauterization is superfluous on medicine by its velocity of health, and the merit of its operation, and the severity of its lordship: and it is possible that he returns to health at some time of time according to the complexion of sickness, and the location of his illness, and the merit of it; and that he was prepared in his body from the accumulation of superfluity in him: and that it has come to itself in acquisition from food, and similar things from causes, unless there is an illness, in which cauterization is administered.
But a slight illness in a small member is superfluous of the excess of moisture, like cauterization of a tooth, for pain, and similar to it; it is possible that he does not return to health in him: that pain. But this happens in few things.
But the words of the people again, that cauterization is the last medicine, are right words: not to what they mean in it: for they believe that there is no cure which confers with medicine, and neither with any other after the occasion of cautery.
But the thing is opposite to what they say, and the intention, that cauterization is the last medicine, is not, except when we administer ways of cure in some illnesses, and they do not confer with medicines: then we administer at the end of things cauterization, and it confers; then from this it falls, that cauterization is the last medicine, not according to what they understand the people and many dull-witted doctors.
Antiqui autem dixerunt, quod cauterium cum auro est melius, quam cauterium cum ferro: et non dixerunt illud, nisi propter aequalitatem auri, et sublimitatem substantiae suae: et dixerunt, quod non facit pus locus cauterii. Yet the old people said that it is better to apply a burn with gold than with iron; and they did not say this except because of the equality of the gold and the poverty of its substance; and they said that there is no place for a burn. But this is not true, for I have tested this and found that it does not work in some bodies without others.
And the cautery with itself is good, and better than that with iron, as they said. But when you apply the fire cautery in the fire from gold, it does not manifest to you, when you set it on fire according to the quantity that you want, because of the redness of gold: and when it comes to its own heat, and if you add ignition to it, it melts in the fire and turns into sheets.
Why does the craftsman fall from that one to work: and because of that it is more expedient and closer for us to make a cautery with iron, out of straightness to the work.
And I too have already arranged this Chapter on the cautery, according to chapters; and I have arranged it from the head down to the feet; that the seeker may find what he wants out of it.
Chapter II.
Here let the cauterization be to the lordship of moisture and coldness on the brain; which two things are the cause of the salt, and of the multitude of flow of humors out of the head to the eyes and ears, and of the multiplicity of sleep, and of the pain of teeth, and of the pain of throat: and universally all sickness that happens from coldness, such as paralysis, and salt, and convulsions, and fainting fits, and those like them from those illnesses.
The method of cauterization is so that you may instruct the sick person: to wash his head with soluable medicine three times a night, or four times, according as the strength of the patient and his age and custom require it.
Then command him to wash his head with cold water. Then make him sit between your hands so that he has already placed his hands on his chest.
Then place the root of your hand over the root of his nose between his eyes. So whichever finger of your middle hand touches the place where the nose joins the face, mark it with a burn.
Then light a cautery with olive oil: whose this is the form.
Then place the person himself on the marked spot with the burn, so that your hand constricts him little by little, and you release him. Then quickly release your hand, and look at the spot.
If you see already a quantity of hair falling out from the bone, or a quantity of herbs, then quickly release your hand. And if not, repeat the ironing of the same with another one when it is frigidized: until you see the bones that I told you.
Then take some salt, and dissolve it in water, and steep it in the liquid, and place it on the spot: and release him for three days.
Then place over the soaked cloth in butter, and release him on the spot until the fire scar is destroyed. Then care for that with the four-part ointment, until it is healed.

Then let the wound be treated until it has healed.

For the head weakens from the continence of its own nature: as we have witnessed in the other members: and especially when the head is weak from sickness; and the species of cautery first are more salutary and better for me, and I use them: therefore let us perform it when healthy.

**Chapter IV. On the treatment of headache.

**But cauterization with iron is according to this method:** The fire cautery, whose form is this.

Then put the iron itself on the spot, and hold your hand: and you shall turn the iron slowly with speed: and let the quantity that is burned from the thickness of the skin be like its half. And draw your hand away, so that the artery which is below does not burn, and do not let the blood flow.
Then dip the cloth in salt water and place it on the spot, and leave it for three days: then dip the cloth with butter: afterwards treat it with ointment until it is healed.

The mode of cauterizing in wounds of the lips.
Often a crack occurs in the lip, which is called a pilus, and especially in the lips of boys.

And let it be pressed down with the sharpness of the knife.

The mode of cauterizing in wounds of the mouth.
When it occurs in the root of the gums, or in the palate, or in the roots of the teeth: then pus is produced, and it breaks: then it dries up, and pus runs, and a fistula forms: then deal with it, and there is no effect from the cure, then you must set fire to the cautery according to the quantity, which enters the fistula. Then introduce the ignited piece into the opening of the fistula, and hold your hand, until the iron is ignited to its depth, and the last part: do it once, or twice.
Then cure it after that, up to the point where we have said that it will be healed.
If then a wound has been incised and healed, it is good. And if not, then it is necessary to remove the place, and take away the corrupted bone according to what comes to its head.
De the mode of cauterizing in gingival diseases, and molified teeth.
When the gum is softened by moisture, and the teeth move, and does not improve with medicine, then place the head of the patient in your lap.
Then, as the shape of the wand comes after these things, so that you put on a cane over the tooth: and insert the lit cautery quickly into it: and hold your hand a little, until the sick one feels the heat of fire already reaching to the root of the tooth.
And dry your hand: then repeat the cautery several times, according to what you want.
Then the sick one should fill his mouth with salt water, and hold it for an hour: and spit it out.
For the tooth is made firm by movement, and the softened gum is tightened, and the corrupt moisture is dried up.
The mode of cauterizing in dental pain:
When the pain of the tooth is because of coldness, or the cold itself is painful, or there is a sore feeling, and the medicine does not improve with drugs: then the cautery must be made in two ways: or with butter-cauterization: or with fire-cauterization.
But the cautery of this is made by butter, as you take butter and heat it up, and pour it into an ear-trumpet or a pair of shears. Then take a wand, and rub it on the tip of the tongue. Then submerge the patient's tongue in heated butter: and place the patient's tongue quickly on a painful tooth, and hold it until it is chilled: then repeat the operation several times, until the strength of fire reaches to the root of the tooth.
And if you wish, dip the thread, or wrap it in cold butter, and place it on the tooth that hurts; and place hot iron upon it, until the fire penetrates deeply into the decayed part of the tooth.
But cauterization with fire, so that you direct it towards the tube of air, or the tube of iron. And let there be some moisture in his body, so that the sharpness of the fire does not penetrate to the back of the throat.
Then take the fire-cauterizer, whose shape you have prepared; and place it on the tooth; and hold your hand, until the cauterizer cools down.
Do this sometimes, for the pain will be relieved, or do it on the same day, or on another day.
And it should be impressed into his bad breath so that it fills the sick mouth with good broth, and he should hold it for an hour: then spit it out.
This is the form of the cauterizer.
Cauterize with either hand as well as you can: and this is the form of the tube.

Which if the apothecary is small: then place the cauterizer according to the quantity of the apothecary.

The way of cauterizing with fire.
When it happens in the liver that there is pain from cold and humidity, or from coarse winds, until it emerges from its natural exit with excess fluid, and the sick person is cared for with what we have said, and does not benefit in him: then it should lie down on a wadded pillow on his back, and sign with caustics three cauterizations according to this form, and this quantity of the same caustic on thespar below the hypochondrius where it reaches the bed of man: and let there be space that is between each cauterization according to the thickness of a finger: and let the cauterization be second to the length of the body straight: and do not compress your hand with the caustic much.

The method for cauterizing the liver.
When a liver abscess occurs and you need to know whether it is located in the liver itself or in its capsule, then if it is in the liver, you will see a painful ache, and there is little pain: but if it is in the capsule of the liver, then you will feel sharp pain; and you will see that the treatment of this hurts doctors.
It is necessary so that the sick person rests on their bed. Then, the shape of the location of the abscess with the caustic: then, the fire of the cautery so that it is absorbed by radiation. And this is its form. And cauterize it with one cautery tool until the skin is completely burned and reaches the nipple, then, take care of the cure for the healed abscesses, until they are healed.
And it is not necessary for this cautery tool to appear in a certain way unless the study of medicine is prolonged: and because of this experience, sometimes some diseases can be treated with great care; then, let one dare to perform such an operation.
But, I prefer to send it away.
The method for cauterizing in pleurisy.
The ancient ones said that the cautery of pleurisy was made with roots of aristaochia, according to this method. And take from the dry stems of aristaochia a long root, which is found in it, and make it be like the thickness of the finger. Then immerse it in oil and set fire to it. Then cauterize with one cauldron between the continuation of the fork with the neck, and two small caldrons little declined on the part that is under the beard.
And two caldrons also in it, that is between the fifth and sixth ribs little declined on the back: and a cauldron other in the middle of the chest, and another above the stomach. And three caldrons behind: one in it that is between two spatulas: and two caldrons on the sides of the chest, lower cauldron that is between two spatulas.
And do not need to squeeze your hand with the cauldron, but let it be in the open skin smelling.
And some ancient people said that who use a cauldron of iron similar in radius: and light it up, and put it inside him that is between the ribs until it reaches with it to the apostema, and comes out pus: as we have said in the apostema of the liver.
But in this piercing with the cauldron is out of fear that either dies weak immediately, or happens in place fistula, which there is no cure.

This mode of treatment is called vaccineum.
§ On the treatment for back pain.
When pain occurs in the back for various reasons, or from accident, or from impact, or from excessive evacuation, and similar cases, with cold and moist material: and cauterization does not fall unless it is done alone in this manner only, which is done with an infusion of cold material.
It is necessary therefore after the evacuation to apply nurses with bitter pills and similar things so as to make them stand on the back where there is pain, three lines according to its width, after the marks have been made by the causticum on each line: five cauterizations, or more according to the quantity which comes from the patient's tolerance of the nurse's virtue: and let cauterization be made with a cauterization needle.
And if you wish, cauterize with three cauterizations, or four, with the middle closed, in this form.
§ On the method of cauterizing in gibbus.
This malady often occurs in small children.
And the sign of the onset of this disease in children is that which occurs on him a stricture in swallowing during elevation and movement: and an inflammation in the last spondylum of the back and its nods, which has already begun to emerge above the other spondyles.
So when you see this and wish to do something about it, then cauterize the affected area with a needle which is a circle in the following form.

§ On the mode of cauterizing in ruptures.
When a rupture occurs in the groin, and the intestine goes down, or the spermatic cord comes close to the testicle, then you should instruct the sick person to discontinue his food in his own day. And let him use laxatives, so that the manure may come out of his bowels.
Then make him sit between your hands on his back; and instruct him to hold his breath until he stands up, or appears in the groin, or moves the intestine.

Then take the caustic fire, which has this form, until it becomes white, and let it throw off sparks. Then reduce the intestine, or the spermatic cord to its concavity. Then let the minister put his hand on the place so that he does not expel the intestine; and you have already opened between the legs of the sick person, and placed under him a pillow, and one turning with his hands, and another holding up his chest, who holds his hands.
Then place the caustic fire on the spot, with your hand holding the caustic standing straight. And hold him, until you succeed with him to the teeth: or repeat with him another motion, if you do not succeed with him in the first attempt up to the teeth.
And carefully observe at the exit of the intestine in the hour of your cauterization, so that you do not burn yourself, and let it go through that into the sick person or death, or great malignity.
And know that when you do not achieve with the cautery the opening of the mouth, your operation does not bring about.
It is necessary that the cautery of infants be subtle, according to their measure.
Then take care with the other ointments, until it has healed.

And let him rest from work, and be filled with food and drink, and with loud sounds.
For when he uses this regimen, so does he heal with whole health.

The way to cauterize during a flow of blood from the artery in the ulnar.
Often a flow of blood comes from an artery which has been cut at the elbow, or at a cut in the skin, or during a member's cauterization, and so on: and it is difficult to remove him.
Then when it has happened to someone of this, then bring your own hand to the orifice of the artery, and place the tip of your finger on it: and hold it tightly until the blood is exhausted under your finger, and does not come out of it.
Then put in fire small oileous cauterizes and many in great number: and blow on them, till they are well burned.
Then take out of them one, or small, or great, according to the thickness of the bone and the place where the artery is broken, and put the cautery on it, then, quickly remove your finger: and hold the cautery, till the blood be stopped.
That if you shall drive away your finger from the opening of the artery with a dry bandage, and put out the fire, then take another fire quickly from those that are prepared in the fire;
So therefore cease not to do it one after another, till the blood be stopped.
And take care, lest you burn up the nerves which are there, and bring on some other mischief to the sick person.

Or put on the part with medicine of its property, which is to stop the flow of blood, and bind it with a compressive ligature.

But if this thing should happen to anyone, and there be no present doctor, nor medicine: then let the finger of the index begin to be placed on the opening of the vein as we have related, and the compression thereof with great force, till the blood which is above the wound be stopped from flowing into it, and over the artery. And the finger should not be moved, above it with cold water always, until the blood has congealed, and thickens, and is cut off.
And meanwhile he should consider whether it is injured by the cautery or by the medicine.

Of the Cure of Water that is gathered on the heads of boys.
Chapter I.
You should know that this disease often occurs in boys at birth: or when the midwife tightens the boy's head without lightness.

So if the moisture is more increased, and the swelling is larger, then you should place them two cracks, following this form.

And after making an incision remove all the moisture. Then tightly close the cracks with rags and dust. Then dip it in wine and oil until the fifth day.

The extraction of the arteries which are in the temples.
Chapter III.
When a man has an old headache, or acute catarrh through acrid humors and heat in the sinuses, or old soda, bitter, and like things: and is treated with the methods of medical treatment, and does not give it up: then we are skilled in these diseases in extracting the arteries from the sinuses, or cauterizing them, as we have told.

Then make bleed from it six ounces of blood up to three times a day.

§ Of the treatment of acute, sharp, and burning tears from the eyes.
When the tears are persistent, and there is a course of tears from the veins that run along the outside of the head's skull: and they do not provide any curative benefit when applied with medicines, and the eyes make the face red, and feel a shivering in the forehead, as though it were shivering like ants: and their eyelids are heated by the acuteness of the tears: then the ancients found cure for them with this operation, and called it the Ense treatment.
And it is, that you shave off the hair from his forehead. Then cut in the forehead three slits equidistant according to the length of the forehead: and make the length of the slit nearly as long as two fingers, and the one slit equidistant in length according to the nose in the middle of the forehead, and the second according to the length of half a nostril, and the third from the other: and take care not to cut the arteries that are on either side: and extend your hand from the continuation of the two jawbones, and make the space between each incision as much as the width of three joined fingers: and have a prepared sponge fragment, or many dry cloths, with which to wipe away the blood.
Then introduce a pair of sharp scissors at the ends of the slits, and whose following form is: From the incision which is from the temporal side, up to the second incision: and scrape off with it all the skin that is between the two incisions, with the caustic, which is on the bone. Then also make that from the middle incision to the second incision. Then also remove this with a chisel, and insert into the first incision again another tool called an acute clement, soft on one side, not acute on the other. And this is its form.
And place his acute side opposite the flesh joined with the skin, and his soft side opposite the bone: and push him in until it reaches the middle crack: and cut off everything with him that hangs down from the head to the eyes: except that which continues the incision up to that which appears from the skin.
Then also make that in the middle crack up to another crack.
And when an equal quantity of blood has run out, drain the places from the clotted blood.
Then also put into every crack a chisel of dried blood, and put on top of them wads dipped in wine, or oil, or vinegar. And be careful so that there is no blistering from the heat.
And on the third day resolve the ligaments, and give a strong infusion with warm water. Then take care of that with basilicon ointment, and of the others which are treated with ointments: until it heals.

Chapter VI.
The care that falls on gold.
All that falls on gold is of four species: either a mineral rock, or a similar stone, such as iron, and rust: or it is from a plant, such as wheat, and beans: or it is a liquid substance, such as water, and vinegar, and things like them: or some kind of creature.
When therefore it falls on gold a rock or kind of stone that does not contribute to the growth of gold, expose Gold to the sun alone: but if you see a stone, then distill into Gold some of the oil of spices, or sesame seed. Then administer the extraction of it, and cause it to be sneezed out with condiments, and sprinkle the nose with dust near the arrival of the sneeze, after you have placed in the circumambulation of the auricle rolls of cloth, or wool, and spread the ear towards the upper part.
For this treatment is often repeated.
But if it does not go out, then administer the extraction of it with a gesticulatory motion, that is, with subtle hand gestures: whose form is this.
But if it goes out with a gesture, it is good.

Then it is pierced with a thin spatula that reaches to the depth of the eye, and the tearful white fluid comes out, and the eye goes down, and is immediately inserted: then tighten it, until it heals.
The Cure of Alcomat.
Chapter 22. On the cure of Alcomat
This ailment called Alcomat, is not anything else but a kind of mucus gathered in the eye, similar to flowing water, and it is not itself.
And the way to operate on it is this, that you make the sick person sit on a prepared seat, or directly facing upwards.
Then take his head in your hands from the two parts, and move it so that the mucus reaches to the lower eyes. Then tighten it, so that it does not fall, and the light is kept in its place: and let the sick person see things as they are: and if it does not go down below, you shall know that it is water.
But if he does not prepare the descent of the wound with him as we have said, then make the sick person sit between your hands. Then take a thin spittle and find with him above the corneal membrane a little at the place of the intersection with the corneal envelope on the crown, until it is discharged.
When it is discharged, then dilute in the eye a hot water in which you have mixed honey: or water in which you have already boiled millet and honey: then let it be cared for with the rest of the cure, until it heals.
As for the care of water that has entered the eye, or a waterfall.
Chapter XXIII
We have already spoken about the kind of water, and what is appropriate for piercing with a revealing and explaining speech, then take that same thing according to health.
Then it is necessary that you make the sick person sit between your hands square, opposite to the light, near the sun: and bandage the healthy eye, and tighten it firmly: then with your hand close to the healthy eye, and with your left hand if the eye in which the water is is on the right: or with your left hand, if the eye is on the left.
Then take with your hand on the right an almond, if the eye is on the left: or with your left hand, if the eye is on the right.
Then put the tip of the almond close to the crown by the thickness of the radius in the same white of the eye apparent tear of a little one: then push the almond with force: and you roll it with your hand, until it penetrates into the membrane of the eye: and you feel that the almond has already reached the watery part.
And it is necessary so that the distance of the almond to the bottom is a measure of space which is from the pupil until the end of blackness: and this is the crown of the eye. You will see it in the same pupil with an eye-watery vision, because of the clarity of the corneal envelope.

When then does it form, and does not rise up: then pull out the almagest with ease: and you relax your hand little by little.
Then dissolve in water a little of the clear salt from the gem: and wash with it the inner eye. Then put outside all the crust, or wool dipped in oil rosined, and wax: and binds with it the healthy eye.
But we, and those who are of our time, put on him crushed cumin mixed with wheat flour.
That if the almagest does not obey you at the entrance to the eye because of its hardness, since it is from men, and whose eye is very hard: then you should take a staff, which is called an alberid, whose this is the form.

And sit fast by his side according to its position until the third day.
Then dissolve it in that dark house, and test his viscus: and let him see things. Then reduce the ligaments until the seventh day.
And it is not fit that he should make that operation at the hour of cure, nor yet immediately after the incision: but rather, let him wait until another day: because the water riseth soon through the rough skin.
But if it happens that the swelling is hot: then it is fit that thou shalt open his eye before the seventh day: and rectify that which is wrong with him, when he doth lie down, until that the swelling hath been healed: then thou shalt let go his water, and put it on his mouth where it goeth under him for some days, while he dwelleth in that dark house.
Then let him go out of the house little by little, and let him turn round in his bed.

Chapter XXIII.
On the care for the flesh that is born in the nose.
When and what kinds of flesh are added to the flesh that is born in the nose, from which something is made that assimilates the scorpion of many feet: and from this is the flesh of cancer, the lapia, of a pale color; and from this is the meat that is soft, not of a pale color.
In that which is of the said meats soft and not deceitful, nor cancerous, it is fit that thou shouldest cause him to sit between thine hands opposite to the sun, and open his nostrils, and put in a sharp hook on the side: then draw them towards the exterior, and cut what thou hast taken from them with a sharp and subtle chisel: then shalt thou know that all the flesh is now removed.
If anything remains that cannot be cut off: then carefully remove it with a fine instrument until nothing is left. If, however, blood is in control of you, or an injury occurs that is warm and swollen: then oppose it with what is necessary; or if it is a malignant apoplexy, then approach cauterizing it, until the blood is cut off, and all flesh is removed.
Then let it be applied to the nose after cutting with vinegar, and water, or wine. If, however, the nose is opened, and moisture proceeds from it all the way to the palate, then you shall know that he is already well.
If, however, the moisture does not penetrate, as it should, then you shall know that within the upper bones of the nose there is a birthmark which the knife cannot reach.
Then it is necessary that you insert a thread of linen which has some grossness, and many nodules in it: and place between each nodule the length of your finger, or less: and let him sneeze, so that he sends one end of the thread into his nose with a ray, or with whatever is possible for him to do after he has done this himself; and let him sniff, until it reaches his throat, and then let him spit it out.
And many times indeed do these boys make this atom similar in schools; and it is a easy thing for him who wants to do it.
Then place the two ends of the thread, one which goes out over the nose, and the other which remains in the nose. Then tie a knot in the thread with the nodules that are in the thread. Make it tight so that you shall know that the flesh is already with the nodules in the thread.
Then pull out the thread: and put into the nose after the removal of blood the liquid that you have imbibed in Egyptian ointment: make it three days, or more, until the ointment has eaten away all that remained of the flesh.
Then place at the end of the thing a cannula of lead in the nose for several days until it heals.
If it does not heal with the cure that dries up, then use that one.
For the cure for nosebleeds, when they are distilled with oil and other medicines into the nose, make it of silver or brass: that it be similar to a small lantern, opened like a shell: and its passage also.
And if you want to make a closed cannula, like a reed.
And the shell of the instrument for the cure of nosebleeds should be full, and well-sealed: whose handle is at the end of it, which you hold when you heat oil in it, or what you want to pour into it, and fluid things.
Chapter 25. On the Cure of Nosebleeds
Nosebleeds often arise at the end of the nose and become more severe and greater with the passing of days until they appear like a wound.
And for this, when it first appears, you should cut it all out. Then place over the spot, or a caustic, or burning medicine that stands in its place.
If the incision is then delayed until it becomes more severe, consider it, if it is hard, dry in color, and of few senses: then do not touch it with iron: for it is a cancerous spot.
For many times water has incised the flesh and led to great evil in that place which had it.
And if the spot is not hard and of few senses, and you see that the incision can be made in all of it, then make the sick person well, and cut him without fear or hesitation: and cure the place along with it, until it heals over.
Chapter 26.
On the sewing up of the nose, lips, and ears.
You should know that when a break occurs in one of the cartilages of the nose, surgery is not effective unless in certain people.
Therefore, when such a thing has happened to someone, you should consider whether it is a recent fracture with its own blood: and then add two wounds together with sutures: and then take care of the wound until it heals.
And if the break has already separated the lips, and some healthy parts have been formed: then it is necessary that you cut off all of its skin from the outside, until it bleeds. Then add two lips together with sutures, and tie them tightly, and sprinkle the wound with dragon's blood, and crushed holly; and put over the powder a fragment of plaster of Paris, and some other consolidated plaster: and let it be bound for two days, or three. Then loosen it, and change the medicine: and let it go, until the hairs grow together by themselves. Then take care of the wound with ointment, until it heals.
The method of suturing is, to add the solution of a break, or with needles, as we have told you about suturing an eye; or to add the solution with a thread, as I have made you aware.
Chapter 27.
On the extraction of nodules from the lips:
Many people have hard nodules in the inner part of their lips, similar to small grains of herbs; and some of them are smaller, and others are larger.
Therefore, you should turn the lips inside out, and find all the nodule, and hang it by a hook, and drop it from all sides.
Then fill the space under the cut with dried blood that has been crushed, until the blood is dried up.
Then clean it with vinegar, and wash the place with it, where there is pain, until the wounds heal.
De incisione carnis natae super gingiuam.
Of the incision born of flesh on the gingivae.
Often a flesh-born wound arises on the gingivae, which the ancients called abluss.
So it is necessary that you should hang her by a hook; or that you hold her with forceps and cut her close to her root, and let the blood run away, or let the blood flow.
Then place on the sore-affected part a dried poultice, or other stimulating powders, drying agents.
That if that flesh returns after cure, it often does so again; then cut it again and cauterize it: for she never returns after cauterization.
De rasione dentium cum ferro, ut neque fac
Of the shaving of teeth with iron.
When the teeth are聚e together in the surface of the tooth from the inner and outer side, and between the gingivae hard tissue, rough hairs, and sometimes wear away, and become yellowed, and white-ish, until the corruption extends to the gingiva, and the teeth become rotten by that.

If then that which is in them from the beginning of shaving is removed, it is good: but if not, then repeat the shaving on them every other day, and third time, until the final study has come to them what you want.

Chapter 30.
Regarding the extraction of pain from teeth.
It is necessary that you treat the tooth with all your skill and quickly remove it from the source of pain. For the tooth should not be left to heal on its own when it is removed, as the substance is delicate: until a time when there is no excuse for the removal of it, then it should be removed carefully, and certified by you to the complaining patient.
For many times pain deceives the sick person, and he thinks that the pain is in the tooth and removes it himself: afterwards, he does not cease to have pain until the tooth is removed from the source of pain.
For we have already seen this from the operation of bloodletting.
When, therefore, the tooth is certified to you by the complaining patient, then it should be scraped with a small scraper, which has some strength, until the gum is completely removed from all sides: then, with your fingers or with delicate forceps, slowly cut off the tip, until you hear it crack.
Then, when you have well positioned the forceps in the good place, and the head of the patient is between his knees: which you hold well, so that it does not move: then, pull the tooth along its straight line, so as not to break it.
If, however, it does not come out, then take one of their instruments and push it in from all sides with ease, and try to move it, as you did before.
If, however, the tooth is either pierced or corroded: then it should be filled with cloth: and you should hold it with a small hook at its tip, so that it does not break when you squeeze it with forceps.
And it is necessary, so that you do not get the scraping around the gum well.
And keep your strength, so that you do not break it, and a part of it remains, and returns to the sick from which greater pain comes at first.
And so, do not do that which the phlebotomators do in their haste, and their boldness in eradicating it, except that they administer to it what we have related.
For often they bring about great sicknesses in men, which are more difficult to be broken, and leave the roots of them all, or some part thereof; or take it out with certain bones of the jaw: as we have been frequently assured that this is done.
Then let it be cleansed after its eradication with wine, or with vinegar and salt.
That if a flux of blood should come from the places (for often it comes) then let some of the plaster be removed and filled with the blood, and let that place be so; and if not, let it be cauterized, if it does not give you plaster.
The shapes of the pincers should be such that they move easily at first, and then do not open when pressure is applied to them on the tooth: they should be like the large fingers, until when you press them they do not open themselves, nor do they open; their ends should be thick, and made of iron or of a hard pewter, straight ends: and in their ends should be teeth, some of which enter into certain ones, and close by constriction, so as to bite strongly.
And when they are made at the ends like the shape of an ear which is called a crow, and are of strong constriction.

Chapter III. On the locking of teeth.
Of the important locking of teeth on others.
When teeth are not at their natural angle, they become rotten from the form of the mouth; and especially when this happens to women and girls, that is, young people.
It is necessary for you to consider. For if one tooth has come after another, and does not fit in place, nor can its lock be made or the grinding be done; then you should cut it out: and if it is joined to a neighboring tooth, then you should cut it with this instrument, of which the following form is.
It is like a small chisel, and is made of hard iron, with an acute end.
And let your cut be deep, so that you cut it over many days, and because of the hardness of the tooth, and so that it does not move one of the other teeth.
If, however, there is a prominent exposure of its grinding: then grind it with a chisel made of hard iron, the form of which is as follows.
It should all be made of iron, and the small point of its tip should be ground off like a chisel, as is done with an awl: so that the tooth can be grating little by little over many days with ease, so that it does not move the teeth, and then allow it to be sharpened at the end, grinding it with some rasps.
If a tooth is broken off, and hurts the tongue: then you should also sharpen that part, until it is smooth, and make it even: and then smooth it out, so that it does not hurt the tongue, nor damage speech.
Chapter III. On the retention of teeth with gold fill.Si D
On the retention of teeth with gold fill.
When there is a blow and jolt to the anterior teeth from a blow, or by accident: and it cannot be held fast to its place, so that it falls out: and it does not improve with careful treatment, then take a sharp wire and retain them with gold: because gold turns green, and spoils after many days; but gold never changes.
And let there be a thread medium in subtility and coarseness according to the quantity that it takes between teeth.
And the form of retraction is such that you take a thread and introduce it between two healthy teeth: then tie it with both ends between them, or one or more, until you come to the other side of the tooth with the thread. Then iterate the texture until the part from which you began: and tighten your hand with subtlety and wisdom, until it does not move at all: and let your constriction be where you tighten the thread, near the roots of the teeth, so that it does not move.
Then cut off the ends of the thread with the forceps: and gather both together, and bend them with your hand: and hide them between a healthy tooth and a moving tooth, so that they do not hurt the tongue: then let it be loosened in such a way as to remain strict.
If however it has loosened, or has been cut, tighten them with another thread, and let it stand with it in such a way for all the time.
And this is the form of the teeth, and the figure of retraction of two healthy teeth and two moving teeth, as you see them.
And when it is that one tooth has fallen, or two behind in its place, and remains as we have described, and does not make it except the craftsman be skilled, prudent, and experienced.
And when one of the ribs is broken: and it is made out of it like the form of a tooth, and is put in its place, from which it is protected, and tightened, as we have said: and remains, and adheres to it for a long time.

The treatment of abscesses of the two amygdales.
Chapter XXXVI.
When abscesses occur between the cheeks, that is, the glands, which are akin to abscesses, and which occur on either side: when cure with the above-mentioned method does not help them, then observe.

And I have already treated a woman with a blisters that had grown between her throat, which was causing hoarseness, little sense, and almost closed her throat: and this woman breathed through my narrowed throat, and had already stopped eating, drinking water, and was close to death if it had lasted another day or two.

On the poultice on the wound.
Chapter 37. On the poultice on the wound.
When a catarrh descends to the wound, and is poulticed, and it is long: then it is called perpendicular; and if it is in the lower part thick, round, then it is called a pouch.
When it is treated with what we said about the separation, and does not bring about a cure; and you see that the poultice has grown cold from it, and has thinned out: then you should hit it.
And what is gathered together on it round, and has no length, and is bloody or dark in color, or black, and has no feeling: then you should hit the incision. For in that poultice there is a fear over the wound.
Then when you see it in the way that I said out of whiteness, and according to its length, so that you set the sick person before the sun: and press his tongue with an instrument, whose predecessor I told you; then fasten a hook on the wound, and pull it down: and hit it with one or two instruments which I said for hitting the two amygdales.
And you should not hit out of it, unless that which is added to it on a natural limit without addition.
For if you hit out of it more, then you wound the voice and speech.

Then hold your hand with the medicine. And you from the first shall put on her on a small quantity of the medicine at an hour's time, until you see it has become black, and has eased the tingling of the medicine.

Do this many times, until you get what you want in the new cauterization. Then dismiss her: for she will be cured and will fall down after three days, or four.
That if you will be cured that you go the medicine, repeat it: and after the cauterization wash about her a new one with the butter, and purify with it what is in her from the medicine: and rinse with cold water.

Then repeat this many times, until it burns.
And do not make this cure at the beginning if there is a wound. For this reason, add it to the wound, and do not make this cure unless it is at the decline of the heat of the wound.
If this instrument is not present with you, then take a tube and fit an end with wax so that it does not burn the nose: for the wax prevents the vapour from burning the nose; and this is good cure with health.
Regarding the extraction of spines and bones from the throat.
Chapter XXXVIII.
Often a tooth or a spine or a bone would adhere to the throat.
Then you should extract what is visible above what causes the pain, when you press the tongue with an instrument against the light and tell me that it is in the throat.
And if what is hidden in the throat does not appear to you, you should make a sick person fast before food digests in his stomach. Perhaps the part attached to the body should exit through the umbilicus.
Perhaps the sick person should swallow a dry piece of bread, or a leaf of lettuce, or swallow a dry cracker: or take a dry sea sponge and consume it; and bind it with a string, and then swallow it. Chapter LII. On the care of the umbilical cord.
On the care of the umbilicus.
To deal with various causes of umbilical care. It may emerge from a fistula that opens on the belly below: or it may emerge from the rectum, according to whether it occurs in the remaining ruptures. Or from blood that flows from a vein, or from an artery, as stated above. Or from a constricted pleat of skin.
If, however, it emerges from a fistula that is clean and the discharge is thick: then it resembles the color of fresh blood: it is soft, without pain: and it appears in various places. And if it is due to the exit of the intestine, then its location is with that which we have mentioned, more varied in character. When you press upon it with your fingers, it is hidden: then it returns, and perhaps has a sound: and it is very prominent at the entrance of the anus, and causes a rumbling noise.
If, however, it is due to moisture, it is soft, and does not hide itself when pressed by your hand, nor becomes tight, nor does it add to itself.
If it is due to blood, then with these signs appears a bruise of a dark color at the tip.
If, however, it is due to raw flesh: then it is a bruise of a gray, thick, hard color, and discolors according to the quantity of one unit.
If, however, it is due to a flow of air, then the sensation of touching it is soft.
And the operation in that case is, as you should consider, if the umbilical eminence is due to blood of an artery or vein or a flow of air: then one should refrain from treating it. For in that is fear and dread, as I taught you in the chapter in which I said that bruises are due to an artery and a flow of air.
If the umbilical eminence is due to an intestine or a hernia, then you should prescribe for the sick person to hold his breath and remain standing with his body extended. Then mark with encaustum around the navel altogether. Then order him to lie down on his back between your hands. Then, with a mallet, beat around the navel from side to side in the place that you marked out with encaustic. Then extend the mid-line of incision upward, with a large hook. Then tie him around strongly with thread or silk knot: and let the knot be tied to an unharmed part of his body.
Then open the mid-line incision that you have extended, above the lymphatic duct. If you have already found the patient's hernia through the lymphatic duct: then pull out the ligature and insert your index finger into him, pushing the intestine towards his insides: and if you find a hernia: then grasp it with the hook, and cut off its surplus.
If, however, the opposite side is an artery, or a vein: then mark him all around. Then return to your operation. And take two needles and insert two strong threads into them: and insert the two needles around what you have marked out around the hernia in the shape of a cross, which you make to penetrate.
Then tighten the hernia in four places on the needles: and if you wish, remove the needle, and leave the place, until the flesh has become rotting: and let it fall down of itself: or cut it off when it becomes slimy and rotten.
Then care for the patient with unguents as needed, until he is healed.
If the navel's eminence is made of flesh that has become hard in it, or of moisture: then you must tighten the hernia, as we have said. And extract the blood or moisture that you have injected into him.
Then care for the patient with unguents so that the place hardens up.

Head L VIII.

Retention of urine in the bladder, occurs from an operation performed by the lodgement of a stone, or congealed blood, pus, or born meat, and similar things, as we have said in the various methods of cure.

Then extend the thread with wool little by little. For the thread follows the wool.
Then pull it out, and let the thread run along the urinary passage: and again push the syringe, and do not cease to do this until the bladder is empty, and feel a relief.

Chapter LXI.
As for the extraction of the stone in women.
Stones are not often formed in women.
If, however, a stone occurs in any of them, then the cure is difficult, and is prohibited on account of many reasons.
One reason is that the woman may be a virgin.
And a second reason is that you should not examine a chaste woman, or one who has a husband.
A third reason is that you should not examine a well-versed woman in this art, especially the hand operation.
And a fourth reason is that she is far away from the stone's location: for fear of injury, and because there is danger in the depth of the incision, and because it is painful.
If necessity compels you to do this, then you should take a female doctor who is well-versed: and there are few of them.
If you really want to do this, then ask for a skilled, subtle doctor; and a well-educated midwife who is familiar with women's affairs, or a woman who is experienced in this art: so that she may do whatever you instruct her to do from your questioning about the stone.

Then she should cut it at the mid-point of the woman' hip opening towards the root, from whichever side it is prepared, and feel the stone in that place; and her finger should not move away from the stone, holding it underneath: and there should be a small section first. Then she should insert the bone end into that section.
Then she should insert the bone end into that section. If she feels the stone, then she should add to that second section a quantity proportional to how much the stone moves away from her.
And you shall know that there are many types of stones. For this one is small and large, hard and rough, long and round, having branches.

Chapter LIX. On the operation for castration.
§ On castration.
Know that castration is excommunicated in our law: and for that reason it would be fit that I should not remember it in my book here: and the remembrance of it is not necessary except for two reasons.
One is that it may be in the knowledge of a physician when he is questioned about it: and that he may know how to cure him who has it happen to him.
And the other mode is that we often lack castration of certain animals, for their benefits to us through it, such as rams, horses, and cats, and like creatures from among the animals.
I say then, that castration is done according to two modes. Or by friction. Or by incision and section.
In that which is done by friction, the course of the operation is: that we should make the animal sit in warm water until its testicles become softened and loosened and hang down: then we should nip them off with our hand, until they are completely gone and do not appear under the touch.
But in castration by incision and section, you should hold the animal, and press the skin of its testicles with your left hand.
Then bind the suspenders, and dry on all the wound one, until both balls have come: then cut it after you have scraped them both, and do not leave behind on it any scurf of skin, which is over the skin.
And this mode of castration is better than that which is done by friction: because by friction there might perhaps be something lost from the testicles, which would make the animal desire to copulate.
Then heal the fracture, until it heals.

Chapter LVII. On the extraction of a dead fetus.

Then she shall be extended with an equal extension, and not be extended only according to the straightness, but let herself move on all sides, so that the exit is relieved: as happens when a tooth is being extracted.

Chapt. XXXVII. Of the instruments necessary for taking out a foetus
The forms of instruments necessary for extracting a fetus.
Form of the scissor which opens the womb.
Here is the form of the scissor, which are equal to the books, and have two tangs at each end of two pieces of wood. But this scissor should be made smaller than the strector; and to make it so, let it be made either of ebony or of bushe-wood.
And the width of each piece of wood should be about two fingers; and the length of each should be a palm and a half, and less than that in the middle; and there are added to each of the two pieces of wood, which are already fastened together, two bits of the same wood, the length of which is a half-palm and a little more; and the width of either is about two fingers, or somewhat less.
And these two bits are to be inserted into the mouth of the womb, so that it may open when you turn the two scissors.
Form of another like that, but smaller and lighter.
Let it be made of ebony-wood or of bushe-wood, according to the similarity of the forceps. But at the end there should be two bits, as you see here above; and the length of each bit should be a palm; and the width of either should be about two fingers.
When you go to open the womb with it, then make the woman sit on the bed, hanging her feet from the side; and Then insert these two bits joined together into the womb; and you shall hold the lower end of the instrument between her thighs. Then open your hand with the instrument, as you do with the forceps, equally according to the quantity that you wish to extract from the opening of the womb; until such time as the midwife does as she pleases.
Form of the other scissor than those mentioned by the ancient authors, which drives out the fetus.
Form the head of the giant: and it is similar to the claw, which has teeth at the end, as you see.
And sometimes it is made round, like a claw according to this form, as you see, which has teeth like the teeth of a saw, so that it cuts and compresses when it is struck and compressed.
Form the hand that holds one nail.
Form the hand that holds two nails.
Form the blade that has two ends for cutting the head.
Form the two blades that also support.
These instruments are all used, and the more their kinds multiply and are prepared for use by the artisan, the faster they carry out their work, and the higher among people due to their quantity.
Therefore do not despise these instruments, although they are found to be prepared by you. It is inevitable that they are needed.

Chapter LXXXXIX.
Regarding the treatment of an untreated wound.
Many boys were born, and the animals of their herd had already been infected by a subtle sifac. It is necessary that the midwife pierce that sifac with her finger: and if not, she herself should pierce it with a sharp spatulium: and she should take care, so that the snake does not touch her.
Then put over it wool soaked in wine and oil. Then take care of the wound with ointment, until it heals.
If you think that it has been poisoned, then put a lead tube in the hole for several days, and let it be removed when the boy wants to defecate.
And also happened poisoning of the animals from the congestion of the wound or from pustules. It is necessary that that congestion be removed, and then it is to be treated with what we have said regarding the treatment from the corruption and lead.
Chapter LXXX.
Regarding the treatment of fistulas which occur in the lower part.
Fistulas which occur in the lower part are a nodulation, and grossness which occurs near the anus of the rectum: or in the space of one part: and it is a fistula, or several.
When therefore that nodulation becomes constricted, it opens, and runs out from it moisture like water, white, or pus.
And when they are from these fistulas, penetrating through. Therefore those that penetrate are recognized by the thing which is excreted from defecation, and air-tightness, when a sick person uses the toilet: and perhaps it is excreted from them naked.
And there are fistulas which penetrate into the space up to the skin, and up to the muscle of the groin. And they are those that penetrate all the way to the hip joint, and up to the tail of the spine.
But from that which is known The penetrating fistula up to the anus, and also from that which does not penetrate, that is, when you insert your index finger into the anus: and insert a fine needle into the fistula made of brass or iron: when there is no curliness.
If there is a curliness in it, then insert into it a fine needle or a shaft from the feathers of an eagle, until you feel the touch of the needle or the shaft on your finger: or a small nail in your finger.
If you do not feel it keenly, and there does not proceed from the opening any discharge, nor is it as I said, dry or scaly: then you will know that it is not a penetrating one: so turn your attention to the cure.
And when the fistula is penetrating all the way to the skin, or up to the edge of the bladder: then the meaning of it is the emission of urine from it, and its prohibition from this so that the place heals with medicines.
But if it is penetrating all the way to the hip joint, or up to the edge of the socket: then the sign of it is that the needle penetrates as far as there, if there is no curliness all the way to the socket: and the finding of pain in the surrounding part, and that pus is always discharged from it: and that it does not scab over with ointment, nor heal with a scabs.
And in these fistulas there is no cure at all: and the cure of them is toil and old age from foolish doctors, who make a lot of noise.
But in that which is not penetrating, and is not old, there is hope of cure with the iron: for I have told you, and I have already proved it: and it is so, that the sick man lies between your hands on his back: and wash his legs all the way up to his thighs, and his thighs are bent towards his belly. Then introduce a lead or air, if it is in a twisted fistula, until you know where the opening lies.
If, however, he feels it to be weak about a certain joint, then it is necessary that you introduce your finger into the joint.
If, then, you feel that the tenting has already penetrated through yourself by detecting it with your finger and between your finger and the skin or flesh: so you should certainly know that it is penetrating: do not therefore labor in it, since it is not a matter of its health, as we have said.

Then treat that with a smooth dressing in butter until the flesh that has been burned out comes off: and then treat it with an ointment that generates flesh and hardens it.
If, then, it heals, it is good.
And if not, then it does not heal with any other treatment ever.
And if it comes out, and does not reach as far as your finger, which is in the anus, and is between the finger and the tenting or a thick layer of flesh, or skin: and you see the fistula in it, following the surface of the skin, then dry the skin from the beginning of the fistula, and you will see that it dries out with the tenting that is in the fistula, until the drying out reaches where the end of the tenting lies, and it is released and falls.
Then clean those filtered flesh that are similar to a feathered tube so that nothing remains of them.
If however the blood is victorious over you, and it flows between you and your work so that it separates those muscles: then it is better if it was done quickly, and with more force of the caustic, cauterize her with fire, or with acute medicine.
For the caustic with fire collects good habits, to the burning of those muscles, and to the separation of blood, and drying up of wetness.

That is, because it is administered in it one of two ways: or as it cauterizes itself, as we have said: or as it separates itself, until the consequences of its concavity come to an end, so that you can insert a caustic, and cure from nearby. Chapter LXXXI.
As for the suturing of hemorrhoids, from which runs blood from the incision of them: and take care of the fissures.
There are hemorrhoids in two ways. Either they are within the anus, like coal or plums; or nearly like grains of grain. And from them there are small ones and large ones: and the blood flows out of them always, and one is, and many are. And they are outside the anus, and at its extremities.
But those outside the anus are few in number and with little moisture, from which flows yellow water or a little blood regularly; and they are the color of the body.
And the treatment that is within the anus, is, as you order the sick person to lie down: and it is expressed, until it comes out of the anus, and appears smooth before you. Therefore hang them with a hook: or hold them with your fingernail. Then cut them at the roots.
But if they do not hold on to the hooks because of their moisture and softness: then take them with a rough cloth or with a twig of willow, and draw them out with your fingers. Then cut them, and sprinkle on them some hot powder after the cut so that they stay in place for a cauterization. Or cauterize them according to what has been said in the chapter of cauterizing. Then care for them with the rest of the cure, until they are healed.
If the anus does not comply with this exit, then clench the sick person with a small clister that causes pain, so that it may go along with what is in the anus, and complies with this exit when it comes out, if it comes out smoothly.
But if the hemorrhoids are external from the anus, then your task is easy and simple: you can hold them with your fingernail or with a hook, and cut them off. Then care for them with the above-mentioned means until they are healed.
And who loathes incisions with iron, then it is necessary that you administer sutures to them in this way. Accept a thread which has been wetted, and introduce it itself into the wound. Then remove the thread to the upper side, and let it penetrate with the needle into the root of its other part: and roll the two ends of the thread, in the lower part of the needle, and let them run parallel to each other: and tighten the thread with a strong knot. Then tie a knot in the thread, and remove the needle.
Do this with all the threads, and let one of them remain: and let it not be attached to its own end, that it may run away from it in case of an excess of blood.
Then put it on a cloth soaked in rose oil or olive oil, and put it on the wound: and order the sick person to lie down. Then remove the cloth, until it has dried.
When therefore the threads have fallen off, care for them with ointment, and with the remainder of the cure, until they are healed.

Head, 84.
I have already said in the division of the healing of wounds which belonged to the book, and I now say here those healing with the hand, and with medicines, according to the second preparation.
I say then that wounds differ according to that by which they are made, and according to the place on which the wound falls.
Therefore the wounds which are often made, are like the blows of a stone: or the cut of a sword, or of a dagger: or the strike of a lance, or of a wood-splinter, or of an arrow. And of many other such things.
Wounds that are made according to the places of the body, such as an arrow that falls on the head, or the neck, or the chest, or the belly, or the side: and similar members from them.
And I am telling about the cures of certain wounds, so that I may put forth a reasoning and a rule for the remaining wounds.

Method of Powder for Burned Wounds. Recipe one part of olive oil, and two parts of dragon's blood, and three parts of burned stone that has not been extinct: all are ground together, and sieved: and it is filled with it, and tightened with a good constriction, until it adheres well to the wound, and clots with blood.
Then tighten it on top with the powder, and leave it, and do not remove it until it has dried up: so that it does not ooze out. For it will not be cured by medicine, until it is cured.
And when it has done this lime alone, when you do not have with you olive oil, and dragon' s blood also does the same operation on many large wounds, when it is put on them and tightened: and are wounds recent with their own blood, which has not been altered by air.
If I have already changed the air by some change, especially when it is in the time of summer, then this powder's purpose is not in this position: therefore take care of him with what we said, so that you put on her some unguents digesting: or put on her some flours from barley, made with water and honey, until they emit pus. Then take care of the rest with this cure, until they are healed.

Regarding wounds of the neck.
If a wound is in the neck, there is no difference in treatment between it and a wound of the head when it is simple.
But if a wound has already penetrated the neck's nerve, or artery: then if it is a nerve, there is no skill in it except to let it run its course with what makes a harsh stipulation, such as vinegar, and dressing, and things like that. Since they are harmful to the nerves, and make them twitch.
Do not put anything cold on the wound at all: since the substance of the nerves is cold, and its continuance is with the brain, which is a noble member: and let this be used with what cure is made from lene, such as calx lota with sweet water sometimes infused with oil, or with rosewater oil, and thistle, when it is rinsed with sweet water: and also the mineral stones, in this way, and wet unguents.

And it is, so that you take an amount, or a number according to the quantity of the width of the wound. Then let go of the end of the fracture by the amount of a finger's length, that is, doctor: and fix now one in front of which you enter into it a thread in the two ends of the skin, with the two ends of the silken thread that is under the skin from the inside, until you make it penetrate from the other side, and you have already gathered the two ends of the skin, and the two ends of the silken thread: and there are four orders.
Then tie it with a double thread around the needle from both sides at the same time, until the two lips of the wound have been gathered together by the falling in: and there are two threads.
Then let go of the grosness of the finger as well, and fix another one in it: then make it straight with a thread, as you did with the needle first. So do not cease doing this with it as long as you feel pain from the spine, until you complete the compression of all the wounds.
And let the points be of a medium size between thickness and slimness: for thin points wound the flesh quickly; and thick ones enter difficultly into the skin: therefore it is necessary that the points be of a medium size both in thinness and in thickness.
And it is not necessary that the point should be placed near both ends of the skin very much: lest the flesh be wounded quickly, and the wound open before its consolidation, and also be stretched with the stitch so that the wound does not be prevented from its consolidation.
Then cut the ends of the points so that they do not hurt the sick person while he is sleeping: and put thereon little pieces of cloth from all sides which shall hold the ends of the points: and let them alone until you know that the wound is consolidated.
And this sort of stitch with points is much more convenient for small wounds. For a stitch of their points is sufficient, or two, or three around them.
But a second common stitch is to bring together the ends of four: the ends of the two pieces of skin, and the ends of the two sutures in one thread, with a point in which the fillet is rolled up of equal thinness and thickness.
Then when you have penetrated with the point the four ends, draw it back from that part from which you began to take it, so that the fillet is stretched from the upper part of the wound: in order that there may be a stitch, as if it were a stitch of a cloth with which the cloth is tied up, and place between each stitch a quantity of thickness of the little finger.
This kind of stitch is known to men.
And with this stitch I sewed up a wound in the man's belly, which had been cut by a knife, and the cut was deep about a handbreath: and there came out of his bowels round about two palms' length of middle intestine, and the cut was in the middle of the belly.
He then reduced that after the intestine had rested outside the pelvis for 24 hours.

But there are two kinds of surgical sutures, which I will describe according to the account.
And it is as if you were to begin with an acute stitch, and to insert the thread from the outside to the inside part.
When the needle therefore penetrates the skin, and goes into the fold according to the length and width of the whole body: release the end of the thread that you inserted from the outside, and let it penetrate the other end from the inside to the outside at the other end.
When you have thus made it penetrate, then let it penetrate according to this in the same end towards the back from the outside to the inside: and release the end of the thread that you inserted from without, and let it penetrate the other end from within to without at the other end.
Then also from this same side, and with the same end, and draw it out of the skin where it penetrated: and take the thread that has passed through the skin.
Then also reduce it into the same skin, and with the other end draw it out of the skin, and take the end that was in the back with this end towards the front: and let it go out of the skin from that part, and make this end move once, and the other move twice: and let it move so as to go round all outside according to this example.
And the outline of this stitch, is as its stitches are sewn together by the skin: so that its one thread goes from one end of the skin; and so that you dismiss it with another thread from the other side until you have finished.
The second type of stitch is a special kind of stitch, which has been described by Galen.
This description of the stitch is told as if it were saying, in accordance with a certain similarity, how some physicians put it together, so that it gathers all its parts in a way that is appropriate to nature: and they apply one end to another end, and one end to another. And this is what I will tell you.
It is necessary that you fix this at the end of the midline near to you from the outside part, and let it penetrate into the inside in that place alone; and dismiss two ends of the thread. Then turn around this again, and let it penetrate from the outside part to the inside at both ends of the thread, that is to say, on either side.
Then turn around both of them, and also let both of them penetrate from the inside to the outside, in two ends of the midline, which are opposite on either side.
And this mode of sewing is a better stitch than an ordinary one, and it is a stitch whose recollection came before.
This is, then, the speech of Galen as well.

The care for Zochaemen, and Fistulae.
Know that all wound, or ulcer when it is old and aged, and turns into a sore, and does not heal, and always discharges pus, and does not close: then it is called generally in any member a Fistula, and we call it by that name Zochaemen.
And a Fistula is indeed by the bitterness of an ulcer, hard, white: which does not cause pain, with which the feathers are bent inwards: and for that reason it is called by some Risati, that is to say, feather.
