Chapter II.
In the prolog to this chapter, we shall state at the outset what is to be considered first in this discussion of the stationing of the members: namely, that you may know and learn this noble art well, or at least gain some knowledge of it, so that those who are zealous for it may learn from you.
I say, therefore, that when it happens to anyone that there is a fracture, or dislocation, or twisting, or a fall: then you should be the first to prepare for phlebotomy on him and to resolve it, or both at the same time: if nothing forbids it, as might happen with the loss of strength of virtue: or is he that has happened to something from those boys, or an old decrepit man: or is the time of a high fever, or a cold fever very sharply.
Then be satisfied with your food, with cold spices and poultry, and with hairiness of the head: and prohibit wine, and fatty meats, and eating too much from dishes, and all food that fills the veins with blood. Until you are secure from a high fever, and do not delay the flowing of the matter to the member: then return the sick one first to his usual customs with which he is accustomed.
When, therefore, the mouth begins to be healed, then you should feed the sick with very nourishing food, dry, and grainy, in which there is a drink: such as is made from wheat flour mixed with water, and rice, and bread, and feet, and entrails of animals, and eggs, and fresh fish: and strong wine, and similar things. For in this regimen the clotting of the fracture is sooner made, and better.
And know that the bones that are broken, when they are in the years of old age and decrepitude, cannot continue always, because of the drying up of their members: and they are strengthened and continued in the end of leniency, such as are the bones of young boys.
Indeed, nature attaches something similar to a broken bone from all parts, which is sticky, which adheres and tightens it until it is glued together on both sides, until it comes to the end of strength and firmness, as it was in the beginning, until it no longer allows itself to be affected by its operations: and for this reason it is necessary that you place food in the sick dishes, in which there are weight, and wetness, and stickiness, as we have said.
And you should know that the types of fractures are various, depending on the diversity of the limbs: for a fracture of the leg is different from a fracture of the skull bone: and a fracture of the chest bone is different from a fracture of the back bone: and likewise each and every limb is different in one way or another: and I came with a reminder of all kinds of fractures presented in its own chapter, distinct from the other. And the species of bone fracture also differ from each other.
Since it is made by a curved fracture, except that which happens in it of the stars. And when a fracture is made according to the length of the bone: and there are fractures of his legs, that is, stars, and additions coming from, such as are in the reed, when it is caught with a knife to write. And not coming from: and there is a fracture with a wound and rupture in the head: and there is a fracture, a small crack.
And every kind has its own tool, or its own proper instrument in restoring it, according to which comes the reminder of it, presented in its own places.
And from this it is known that the bone fracture is, its curve and eminence: and its appearance to the sense, and its sound when you press it with your hand.
When it is not in place, the curve appears visible, and its eminence: and its appearance to the sense, and its sound when you press it with your hand. So then is there no fracture there, but it is possible that there is a curve or a small crack: so you do not need to move it at all with your hand and compression, but rather place something on top of it from the medicines, of which the reminder comes, after a moment that it fits in place: then tighten it tightly with a slender band.
And you should know that when the part is arched and divided into two, apart from what happens there in terms of satagiae, all parts are moved away from their comparison: then it is necessary for you to start from your hour to rectify it, making it even before it happens apostema hot.
If, however, it happens to him that apostema is hot, then leave him for a few days until the apostema is calm. Then make it even in any way you can from slowness and ingenuity.
And you should know that the restoration of him and his equating is easier than the bones, where satagiae happen.
Then tighten it, according to what will come to your mind about him.
If, however, the satagiae are in the bone, then it is necessary to extend this broken member of two parts, if the hand is there, or the foot: or with your hand if the member is small: or with two ropes, or with a rope and a hand.
And your position should be that you place the member on an equal place, according to its natural figure, until all parts of the broken member are extended: then try, with all your ingenuity, to bring it back into shape in any way you can in its places: and carefully, according to your strength, so that no pain happens on the weak one through your operation, nor over-exertion: and be careful, according to your strength, that you apply one of the two bones to his partner, according to the better forms.
And it is necessary in that hour that you try and touch him with your hand. If, however, you see something different there, then make it even, and make it equal by the amount of your strength.
And be careful about not extending too quickly, and do not compress too strongly, as many do with the stoics. For many times they cause to happen through their operation its hot apostema, or contraction in a member: as we have already seen that from their operations.
Then after resting, and letting the wound heal, and tightening his member, and quietness, and tranquillity; and remind the sick man about himself. And let the man be careful, so that he does not moan in his hour of need, and his sleep is disturbed during his operation, and his agitation during his digestion, and all his movements at the end of his strength. And so that he may be careful, that the position of the member does not remain in one place with which pain is caused. That is, for when he feels pain in the position of the member's disposition, or injury: then one should turn him over to a position from that with which he does not feel pain.
And be careful with him, so that his position is equal and straight: so that twistedness does not occur in the member when he is healed.
And the quality of a tight member that has been broken is according to the width.
You should know that broken members differ in their length and form, and their size.
What are shorter ones, such as the arm, finger, or paw, then one should make the wrapping soft and thin. What are the bulkier ones, such as the hip or leg, then one should make the wrapping thick and hard.
For a broad ligament joins a large member and ties it together with an equal tightness so that it does not enter the member in any spot.
Then after you have evacuated your discharge from the equating place put a suitable liniment on it, with a soft cloth over the break site. Then start to wrap the ligament over the break site three or four times, as the member deserves it; and grip your hand lightly with the ligament.
Then walk with him to the upper side from the break spot, and tighten it less than your tightness that you tightened the broken place. Then, as you extend it with an involution from the site of the fracture, and soften the rigor gradually, until you have taken away from the place a large part of the sick member.
Then take another one, and extend it again over the site of the fracture with involutions. Then carry it with an involution to the lower part from the fracture. And let your operation continue on the rigor of the involution and its softening, according to what we have said in the first superior involution.
Then place between the involutions of the cloth or fabric with which you even out the rigor, if there is any curvature of the fracture: and if not, do not place anything in it.
Then extend again on the other taken one. Then immediately set equal to these involutions of stars made out of place, if there is no swelling or fever in the place.
But if there is an inflammation or a swelling: then place over it what sits that swelling and takes away the inflammation: and let it go for a few days. Then tighten again on the same stars when the swelling has gone.
And let the stars be made from the middle of the broad boards, rasped, and formed wisely: or from wood from which are made cribs, that is to say, branches of a palm tree, or a gorse, that is, branches of a holly: and let the width of each star be according to this form, and this figure.
But it is necessary that the star placed on the wound should be thicker and wider about than the other stars: and that the length of the star should be according to what is suitable for the member from size and proportion.
Then tighten again on the stars with another one, according to the same rigor. Then bind from above with the fils made out of place, according to what we have said about the rigor: and it is, that your rigor should be on the site of the fracture more: and the greater it is extended from the fracture, the smaller is the rigor.
It is necessary that they are fine threads in thickness and fineness, and they are from soft linen.
For if the threads are thick, as is the case with the sutures that surgeons place their threads from twisted linen cords, there is a great mistake, for constriction falls outside the quality.
And fine threads also do not agree: for you do not achieve that which you wish with constriction, if they are there.
And it is not necessary that it be between a thumb and a finger.
That if a sick person is injured through the ends of the sutures in healthy places: then place under them from soft stuff, or red wool, until there is no injury from it.
But when there is a fracture with an ulnar bone, and there is a rupture in the skin, I remember it in the second chapter with singularity.
And you should know that not every broken limb needs to be stitched from the first day. That is, because the limb is large when it is broken, it is not necessary to place the top sutures, unless after five days, or seven, or more, according to your security from infection.
And this is the way that ancient people used to patch, which are placed on a fracture and dislocation.
The common shortened way of the patch, restoring a fracture, suitable for many compartments, especially for women and children, because it is from this that heat does not affect, nor cold.
And it is so that you take from the flour which is ground, and it is a meal hanging on the walls of the mill when moved by the wheel: and shape it as it is, except that you add white wax to it, and do not make it dense or thin. Then use it.
The other way of the patch, which restores a fracture and a dislocation, and twists. Brew meslin, and laurels, and acacia, and poultices, and myrrh: and suck all of them the juice of one pound of myrrh, and five of aloes, and twenty of alethic resin, let everything be well shaken, and let it be mixed with water of alethic resin: or with alumine, if the complexity is hot. Then use this plaster. For this reason, good composition is, when broken bones are mended quickly and gives strength to many people.
The method of applying the plaster is as follows, which also helps in the healing of fractures and dislocations. Brew myrrh, and meslin, and alethic resin, all of them the juice of ten pounds of myrrh, five of aloes, six of acacia, and twenty of Armenian bole, let everything be well shaken, and let it be made with water or alumine, and let it be administered.
The method of applying the plaster to injuries and separated bones from their sites, and to calm pain in an injury, to heal broken joints, and to mend a broken bone. A soaked wool is placed, and immersed in vinegar and oil, and is placed on the spot.
In this plaster, there is no power of restoration, but it is good for calming a hot swelling: and it drives away pains on its own.
The method of applying the plaster, which restores a broken bone. Soak leaves from an oak tree and leaves from a wild papaw; shake them all up, and make the plaster out of both.
The method of applying the shortened plaster, administered to restore a broken bone: and when you wish to resolve a swelling that is present. Brew the root of alethium, and chamomile, and flowers of wormwood, and herb-grain, all of them one part; let everything be well shaken, and let it be made with wine, if it is not a hot member.
If it is indeed heated, then then apply wet with coriander seed water, or water, and use it.
A stronger method of application in the case of a hard dry sore when the member is to be restored, namely: Take the root of aloes, and linseed, and bilberry, and mallow, and samsuc, and flower of caraway, all in equal parts; these are to be well pressed together, and applied with oak-bark water, or sweet water, or wine. All this is to be used according to the heat of the member, and the quieting of its heat.
Hippocrates indeed in his book did not say that a broken member should be placed on a poultice made of straw and oil: nothing else; and he tells us to use it when the member is neither too hot nor too cold.
But Galenus, however, used to apply to the broken member for its restoration certain things in which there is a drying up of heat, such as myrrh and aloes and olive wax and similar things of which we have spoken.
A method of application that is useful for wounds and pain. Take mug-wort, and chickweed, and hair of man's teeth, or hen's feathers, and aloes, and salt; all in equal parts: press them together, and rub them up, and make an ointment.
But as to the quantity, according to how much should remain of it for it not to run out, and then to dissolve, you are to consider that if there is no pain, or itching, or movement in the broken jaw from its place: do not break up the plaster over a few days. If there should be a very painful itching, or an inquieting pain, or swelling: then at once break it up, and dissolve it at the same time, and take away the plaster from it; and take a piece of linen cloth, or a sea sponge, damp with water: and immerse it in warm water, and wash the place with it, until the itching is calm, and the pain subsides.
If there should be a very painful itching, or an inquieting pain, or swelling: then at once break it up, and dissolve it at the same time, and take away the plaster from it; and take a piece of linen cloth, or a sea sponge, damp with water: and immerse it in warm water, and wash the place with it, until the itching is calm, and the pain subsides. Then release the limb to rest for an hour. Then cover the wound with wool, soaked in vinegar and oil, or oil infused with rose water; and bind it to the body for a night, until the fever has subsided, and the inflammation of the member has settled, and the pain has abated.
Then massage the wound with a mild emollient; and do not bind it tightly at first, nor use tight bindings, until the wound has healed.
If you see that the swelling, redness, pain and inflammation have completely disappeared, and that the wound is well covered by the poultice and bound firmly, then apply the poultice again as before.
If any of these things do not occur in the member, then do not remove the poultice until after three days, or four, or five, or seven. And when the poultice has been left for twenty days all over the wound, as far as appears from the healing of the member, so that it is firmly attached to the fracture, and the blood clottings are approaching; add also then more than your first constriction; and add also then an increase in the food diet, as it were by reminiscence.
But if you see that the place of the fracture is drying up and growing weaker more than it should: then know that food is forbidden from reaching it. So soak it with warm water at every hour, and keep it covered for all three days, and allow a little bit of air to the wound.
By this means, therefore, the member is supplied with food, and heals quickly.
But this, that is done by dull healers from a broken member's fracture, when not done first, as it should be, and done according to the tortuosity of the break, is an error in their work, and a great fear: and if it were right, the ancients would have written about it in their books, and would have worked it.
And I did not see anywhere their footprints at all, and it is better, so that it is not done.
§ On the making of an accident in the head.
The kinds of fractures are various, and their forms are many: and the causes of them are distinct.
For this type of fracture is made from impact when the weapon strikes and the impact carries on until it reaches the bone's syphacus, which is beneath the bone, as it does in wood with an axe: and for this reason this type of fracture is called axial.
Or a weapon cuts into some bone and cuts its face, but the cut does not penetrate to the very end of it: and this type of fracture is called a kalaan, that is, an absolute lifting.
And there is a joint of two fractures, or large, or small.
And one type of fracture is a crushing, or wearing away: and its cause is impact with a stone, or falling on a stone, or similar things.
And this type of fracture is also piercing, which is beginning to approach a flap, which is beneath the bone: or is in the face of the bone: and this type of fracture is also a joint of two fractures, small or large.
And one type of fracture is hidden in a bone in the subtlety of a hair: and it is a small crack, and because of this it is called this type of fracture Capillaris.
And from these is a fracture which is made from falling or striking with a stone, and from things similar to these, which makes the bone's lamina go inside, and it is joined by concavity: as happened in the case of caldarius' wind, when it was struck by them, and some of them went inside.
And many things are made from this in the heads of moist bones, as are the heads of boys.
And all these types of fracture are separate from each other, and I come with a reminder of all this in its place.
We know all these kinds of fractures by examination on them and by questioning about them with tension, and by removing the corrupted flesh which is on them.
Indeed the capillary species is recognized by this, which is discovered on the bone and abraded and kept on it with pitch: for the fracture appears black.
In the cure of fractures, consider first the symptoms of the disease: if you see from these that which signifies a clear meaning over fear, such as fainting with cholera and enlargement and loss of intelligence and cutting off of speech and syncope and acute fever and outgoing of the eyes and their redness and similar things from among these in various dispositions, there is no doubt that he will not be cured: and if you see non-terrifying symptoms in him, and you hope for his recovery, then begin to treat him.
That is, if he comes to you wounded first with a wound: and this happens in the middle of winter, then one should seek, so that you study in the removal of the bone before the sixteenth day of December according to every disposition.
And if it is in the days of summer, then one should seek to remove the bone before the seventh day, before that which is under the bone from a flap rusts, and those accidents which we have described.
That is, if the fracture of the bone has already reached the skin touching the brain, and it is with a little, and attraction: then one should seek to hit the broken part, crushed and ground, according to what I tell you.
And it is, as you shave the head of the wounded man: and discover on the bone according to any possible way for you: and according to the figure of the wound, and what is whiter is over the weak.
That is, if it happens to you with the detection of the bone of a flow of blood or hot swelling: then oppose it with that which is necessary. And it is, as you fill the place with cloths soaked in wine and rose oil: and make the wound rest, until the swelling subsides, and be safe from the flow of blood. Then begin with the cure of bones and the removal thereof.
And that is done according to two ways.
The mode which is one, is that you should strike the bone with an incision of a sharp chisel; and this is its form.
Then give another chisel, wider by so much as the first; and this is its form.
Then also give another chisel, wider than the second.
The second mode is that you should have at your disposal a number of chisels of various widths; some of these shall be wider than others, and some narrower: and they shall be on the extremities of their edges, sharp from iron or fuel: and use convenience in striking upon the chisel, so that it does not strike the head and harm it.
If the bone is strong and hard: then you should make a hole around it before you administer the chisel, with a twister, which they call a tweezers, that does not go to the bottom of the flesh towards the bone: because the tip is round on that part which is under the acute head, like the edge, and a little circle prevents it from sinking into the thickness of the bone.
And it will be suitable for you to take from these tweezers a large number, the quantity of which corresponds to the thickness of the bone, until you have all the chisel tips needed: whose acute tip is in length and brevity according to the quantity of its bone thickness.
And this is the form of the three types of tweezers, or trepans.
The quality of the tip that pierces around the broken bone is: you should place the tweezers on the bone and roll it with your fingers, until you know that the bone has been pierced. Then change the tweezers to another place, and place whatever is between all the holes of its width, or around it.
Then cut with the incision what is between all openings from bone: and make it finer than you can on it from the end of levity, as we have said, until you get rid of the bone, or with your hand, or with some other instrument that you have prepared for it: such as tenals and subtile forceps.
And it is necessary, so that you take every precaution, so that it does not touch the temporomandibular joint, or any of the incisors from the syphons.
When you have circumcised the jaw while perforating it, and if it is separated from the syphons, if it is continuous with it, and you remove it, it should be rough, and the same end of it that remains in the bone with another similar incision instrument. However, it should be fine and finer than other incision instruments.
If there remains something of the bones small, and they are soft: then take them easily with you from those that are with you from the instruments. Then care for the wound after that with liniments, and the ointment which we have said.
And another way of doing this is simple, far from fear: which Galen said, and praised him highly: and this is his saying. You must necessarily, first of all, so that you start to grasp the bone's part in which the fracture is more painful and more evident: until when you have grasped that part, and placed under it the end of this lenticular incision instrument, whose this is the form.
And the lenticular part, soft does not incise anything: and the acute part of it on its two sides washing along in length, as you see: so that the lenticular part is placed against the syphons, its acute part facing the bone.
Then beat over the incision from one part with a small mallet, until you incise all the bone with ease, as it rotates: and you are safe from the panic that there does not happen to it an accident, which you are certainly afraid of.
If a person were not foolish, and more ignorant of it, and not sleepy, then if something was left attached to the bone from the pannicle in certain places, he would hit himself with the end of the lens incisor, and free himself easily. For it is freed from him without harm, and without fear.
For if the fracture of the bone does not go through to the pannicle, and what is cut off from the bone is the face of it, and there remain in him roughness and subtle contusions: then he should make smooth this roughness and pluck out these contusions with fine razors which you have prepared different quantities, so that it becomes possible for you to administer them in any place convenient for them, and straightened out their form according to what draws you into the operation, and the broken bone, and its form. And it is necessary that you administer in your ratio, first of all the root of the bone, with a wider blade of these razors. Then use after this the finer edge of the tool.
And it is necessary that you administer in your ratio, first of all the root of the bone, with a wider blade of these razors. Then use after this the finer edge of the tool. And do not cease to make this according to continuity, until you come to administer them, all thinner and finer.
In each and every of the small cracks left behind in the skin, and in the subtle fracture, it is necessary that you administer care, according to what draws you into its rectification.
And this is a thing that does not hide itself above all, which in this art is any study, and stands on what we have written and shown in large fractures.
But if the pannicle is caught, when you cut off the bone: then it is necessary that you take linen cloth, according to the quantity of the wound, and dip it into rose oil, and put it on the opening of the wound. Then take another cloth, doubled or trebled, and dip it into rose wine and oil, and put it on the first cloth: and make it so that you can do this more easily than the other, so that it does not stick to it.
Then apply the medicine above the wide ligament: and do not tighten it, unless only so much as the tendency of the fracture: and let go with this cure on the first or second day, until you are free from the heat of the apoplexy. Then dissolve it and apply the medicines afterwards which are useless without moisture: such as the roots of lilies, and the flour of herbs, and the powder of thorns, and of arisolochia, and like things: and make from these medicines a powder to be applied on the fracture as you do with those which are dry.
And universally apply in it all medicine of its kind, for it will purify and not hurt. And let it be, according to your skill, that the fracture be clear in your cure: and may it not be in it the grease of an unction, nor any dirtiness.
And let it be, according to your skill, that the fracture be clear in your cure: and may it not be in it the grease of an unction, nor any dirtiness. And do not let any pus accumulate at all in it: for when the pus gathers on the skin of the brain, it corrupts and spoils it, and causes a great evil after it: and this already happens in the scalp, if it is cleaned from it by neglect of its cure, especially by blackness on its surface.
Consider therefore then, if these accidents happen to the sick person which we have said, that it is certainly dangerous.
And if the blackness does not happen except from the medicine which is applied on it, and there is in its power that it should cause it: then you must take from honey one part, from rose oil three parts, and with them linen cloth. Then put the fracture on the scalp: then cure it with the species of cure which are fitting, until it is healed.
De restoratione nasi when it is broken.
You should know that the two upper parts of the nose do not break unless at the same time: for each one is a bone: since the lower part is cartilage only, it does not break and does not happen to it except by chafing, and twist, and like things.
If one of the two parts is broken, then it is necessary to insert a small finger in the nose hole and equally break the fracture with your index finger and thumb until the nose returns to its natural shape: and do this artfully and easily. And take care not to cause that pain on the weakened one during your operation.
That if the fracture is in the upper part of the nose and does not reach that finger: then it should be evened out with the tip of the radius, in which there is a small thickness.
If the fracture is in two parts then make it similar: and proceed with its restoration on the first day of the fracture if possible. And if not, then after seven or ten days until it has healed with the heat of rest.
Then insert into the nose's tip a linen thread if it is broken on one side: or insert two threads if it is broken in two parts: and let it be of such a thickness as to fill the nose hole.
And some old restorers said that they should dip the thread into butter and change it every day. That does not seem right to me, but it should be dipped into the chalk powder when it is finely ground with the flour. Then remove the thread until the nose bone has hardened and the cartilage has become tough.
And when a goose feather is inserted into the nose place instead of the thread after it has been wrapped with a soft linen cloth: and it happens that it holds the fracture in the nose more securely, and does not prevent the weakened one from breathing.
This is not necessary. If you want to do so, then do it: and if you don't want to, then do the thread.
That if the nose should get warm again during the operation while healing: then put a green poultice on the nose: or dip it in rosewater infused with a little honey, or a little bit of dandelion.
If, however, an aposteme is not hot, then one should make a paste of it with sissa flour and a dust of turpentine mixed with alumen. Then place a wooden bandage on top of it, and do not bind the nose at all.
If, however, the nasals are broken or crushed, then one should cut them above and remove them with the instruments that are suitable for this. Then carry out the incision, and take care of the flesh that has become inflamed and consolidated with the plaster that is suitable for it, or with a ointment.
If, however, an ulnar bone is broken between the nostrils, then one should heal it with liniments: and administer a copper tube, until it has healed.
For restoring of the lower jaw, when it is broken.
When the lower jaw is broken and not joined to the ulna: then if it is a simple fracture, and not one that breaks into two pieces, and care is taken of the interior: then it is easy to know.
Then, if it is a fracture on the right side of the jaw, insert your index finger of your left hand into the nose: likewise, if it is a fracture on the left side of the jaw, then insert your index finger of your right hand: and push it with this towards the outside from the inside with your other hand from the inner side of the bone that has been pushed out of place, and so adjust it evenly.
If the jaw fracture has already separated into two pieces, then one should adjust the outward projection of the two parts according to its straightness: until it is possible to adjust it evenly.
If, however, an injury has already occurred to the teeth, or a dislocation or fracture: then tighten them with the help of gold, silver, or silk thread that you hope will remain with them. Then place a poultice on the fractured jaw. Then place over it a double cloth, and place over that a large dish-cloth, poorly made: or a piece of skin of the sole, equal to the length of the jaw.
Then tie the cloth above it, according to how you have prepared your bandage and let it remain in place until it is destroyed: and tell the sick person to use it with tranquility and calmness: and give him a soft food for his digestion.
But if you think that something of the shape is changing with some kind of change: then come close to it and wash it on the third day. Then straightened out what has been changed from it with flour for grinding, mixed with alum or wheat flour, after you have removed the scabs: and place over it a thick woolen cloth.
While that thin plaster remains attached to the wound, and the bone arrangement does not change, let go of it, do not tear it off, until it heals: or tighten the fracture. This fracture is often tightened seven times.
But if during this time there is a hot blister, then administer to it what we have said many times in its sedative, until it disappears.
And if the fracture is with the ulnar bone, consider. I have already said that the fragment is separated from the bone, or from the spondylosis: then in this you are careful, so as to remove those spondylosis with it when it comes to you in the removal of them from the instruments.
But if the orifice of the ulnar bone is closed, then open it with a phlebotome according to the quantity of your need.
Then when you remove those spondylosis, and there remains none of them, then make the orifice of the ulnar bone wider if it is wide: and if not, place over the wound that plaster which comes with it to the wound, and let it grow into flesh until it heals.
As for restoring a fork, when it breaks.
The furcula is broken from the anterior part of the shoulder blade, and the break occurs in one of three ways.
Or it may break and separate into two pieces, except that they happen to be in a state where the bone is still attached: and it is easier to repair it.
Or such that they happen to the bone of the arm: and it is more difficult to repair it.
Or such that it is a break with the ulna bone.
And the operation in it when the break is without an ulna, is, so that you have two ministers who hold one of them to help with the broken furcula, and the other extends the neck towards the side.
Then you make the fingers of your hand even, until it is shaped like this, as follows should be: and there should not be any protuberance or depression in it.
That if you can bear an extension more, then you should put a round ball under the titillium (small of back) from cloth or wool: and let the size of it be according to the need: and extend it, and smooth out the furcula, and clamp the ball with your hand, until the break is even, as follows should be:
That if you cannot draw the tip of the furcula to the outside: since it goes down to a depth: then it is necessary that it should lie on its back on its shoulder blade: and you place under its shoulder blade a ball from wool in size, and press the minister of his shoulder blade downward, until he lifts up the bone of the furcula which is deep: and then adjust the break straight, and make it even with your fingers.
If however you feel a pain from the furcula, and it twitches: then you should find on it, and pull out the alsathaiatum (soft tissue) with ease.
If however it is stuck to the bone, then use your incision instrument which you have prepared for it, after you have placed under the furcula a device that holds it tight: and this device is made of wood or iron. And this is his form.
It is assimilated to the ear which is not congenital.
And its width according to that in which you stand so from the size of the bones, and the height of it. The length as well is according to what is possible for you to operate.
And it has two ends, as you see: and the one end be wide, and the other strict.
If then the rupture of the wound which you split when you extracted the bone-set is wide, and secures from the hot apothema: then add two lips of the wound with a suture.
And if the rupture is small, or if you fear the hot apothema: then fill the wound with linen, and dust it according to the quantity of the wound.
That if it should happen that the apothema is hot, then fill the linen with oil of rose, and vinegar, and wine, and set it on itself.
The stricture of the bone is, when it is without wound, and without section, is, as you put on os an olem made from flour pounded with alabaster dust. And set a block of wood on it. Then set a ball under its tip, if you can bear that. Then take the hematic fluid, which is a ball held by the Saracens, which is long far more than it is wide, and its width a palm or so, or about that. Then set a doubled powder on the block, and the plaster. Then take an arrow from a thin board, whose width is three fingers, and in length similarly. Then wrap it in a cloth. Then wrap the cloth with the arrow in place, on the fracture and spot. Then set the alichemist on the fracture as a bandage, and wrap it around his neck, and under his chin, and over his collar bone, and under his armpit: and let it be tight, so that the stricture does not shift: and this is, that it should not hide from you, and all, so that the arrow does not move from above the bone's fracture. You should look at the sick person every day. As soon as the ligaments have softened and you see that the bone has moved, then straightens it and ties the ligaments tightly.
Then put an anaesthetic poultice on his back, and place a warm cloth under his thigh while he sleeps at night, so that when he rises his relief will be greater. So the fracture of the femur is healed by the elevation of the shoulder.
Or bind his arm to his neck; and do not untie the ligaments unless there is an accident from a sprain or a fall until after twelve days.
Then put on a poultice of mud, if you see that his face is red to it. And add salt, and let him go, until he has recovered and has coagulated.
And the fracture of the femur is bound up and made firm after thirty days; and sometimes it heals sooner in some people.
On the restoration of the fracture of the spatula.
The spatula rarely breaks and at its broad end; it does not break from its broad end.
So if it breaks from one end, and it breaks in the middle, and it is not known until you feel it; then prepare the treatment according to the shape of the fracture. And reduce it to its natural position by all the skill at your command, which is possible for you.
Then put on the wound a poultice of flour mixed with chalk, and a wooden board, and place a warm cloth on top of it. Then put on the person a thin pad of hay from a thin board according to the length of the spatula throughout, or longer it by a little.
If the person is under the pad in any part of the spatula, then equalize it with the wooden board, until the pad drops down according to the equality.
Then, with the help of an alchemist, make a long strip of cloth tight and firm, and fasten it to the body, until it does not move from its place.
And consider the ligature every day. Whenever it becomes soft, tighten it again and if it moves from its place at all, make the fabric equal in thickness and let the sick person lie on his sound side.
And the spatula is to be restored after twenty or twenty-five days.
When this number is complete, then loosen the ligature: and you shall be safe. For this is made of bones whose connection is not feared, and their loosening causes no harm.
But if it proceeds from a bone-fragment, and pierces the skin: then cut it above it, and remove it. And do as I said in the fracture of the fork from sitting on a hot seat, if anything happens from it.
Regarding the restoration of a fractured breastbone:
The breastbone sometimes breaks in the middle, and this rarely happens. Its extremities, however, are most likely to break, and they are destroyed by the accidents that befall it.
When the middle of it breaks and turns downward, there is a painful difficulty in breathing, coughing, perhaps a little blood, and a broken sense; its restoration is that the sick person shall lie on his back: and place the spatula under its bones.
Then press his shoulders and join the ribs on both sides with the hands: and make it equal in length, in whatever way is possible, and let it come together; until it恢复 its shape, according to which it is needed, let it be set in place.
Then place a plaster and a pad on it, and put the board above it from a thin piece of linden or poplar, and something similar for lightness, after you have washed it with a cloth. Then apply a delicate ligature over the broken bone to keep it in place, and sometimes turn it slightly backwards so that it may not move forward; and tighten the ligature as the skin tightens around it.
Then consider the ligature at all times, and whenever it becomes soft, tighten it; and if necessity compels to undo it for any reason, such as itching, or corrosion that has occurred in the place, or pain, or swelling: then approach and loosen it, and remove the plaster, and set aright what has happened during the course of treatment.
Then remove the plaster, if you see towards it: and attach to it the ligature, until it is healed.
For the restoration of bones when they are broken.
You should know that the fracture of bones does not occur unless in the areas behind the back, and from the extremes thereof towards the front part. And it does not occur to them any wearisome thing, except therefore, because they are cartilage-like. And the knowledge thereof is not concealed from the sense during inquiry with the fingers.
And the restoration thereof is done in such a way that the break between the fingers is made even, according to what is possible, so that it may be even in shape; and according to that it needs to be. Then wrap a plaster around the broken bone with a splint if necessary.
And if the fracture of the bones is hidden towards the inside: then a very painful pain happens to the person, and a kind of tickling, such as that which happens to him who has pleurisy; because the bone pricks the wound, and also difficulty in breathing, and coughing, and much blood-spitting: and this is difficult to treat.
And the ancient people have made many inventions for it; and among them are those who say that you should put on a bed that causes inflammation and irritation, so that the belly may become inflamed, and spread out, and push forward the fracture to the outside.
We therefore abhor the formation of a cold poultice, lest it cause a delay in making an warm one if it does not occur. But if it has already occurred, then let us add to that.
And one of them said: Let a warm place be placed there, and then let it be drained with strength, and it is more like reasoning. However, we fear that the warm vapours will attract fluid to the spot, due to the weak condition of the body.
And one of them said: It is necessary that the place be covered with wool which has already been immersed in hot oil: and let feathers be placed between the ribs, so that it be filled: so as to be a loop of equal length when it is turned around by its roundness.
Then let the sick person be cared for with pleurosis from food and medicine.
That if we strap the sick person tightly, which it does not tolerate, and the mouth is pricked by a pin that hurts, and we fear for the sick man: then let us split him on the spot, and let us find from the ribs a broken one. Then let us place over it an instrument that preserves the siffar, whose memory preceded: and we will cut the mouth with ease, and we will cut into it.
Then let us gather together two lips of the wound, if it is large with a stitch: and let us treat him with plasters, until he heals.
That if the warm poultice occurs during this time, then let us approach, and pour feathers into rose oil, and place them on the spot: and let the sick person be treated with it as is appropriate to the poultice.
In the meantime, he shall also lie down on the side where he sleeps, until he heals.
The restoration of a broken spine of the back and neck.
Of the bones of the neck, when a fracture occurs, and it rarely happens that they are worn out: and so also of the spines of the back. So then if it happened to someone and you don't know whether it was cured or not, trust. For if you see his hands already softened, and stuporized, and dead: and cannot be moved, nor extended, nor constricted: and when you constrict them with your fingers, and pierce them with a needle, he does not feel it, and does not feel pain in them: then you will know that it is not healed, and it is deadly in many dispositions.
That if he moves them, and feels constriction with your fingers and the prick: then you will know that the neck of the bones is healed, and that the sick will be healed with cure.
That if it becomes like the spine of the back on him, and you want to know whether it is healed or not, then consider his feet: then if you see that they are softened, and what happened to his hands happens to them: then when he lies down on his back, he moves and has a desire to defecate without volition: and when he raises himself up over his belly, he does the same: and when he raises himself up over his back, and wants to drink, and cannot: then you will know that it is deadly. So do not work on his cure.
But if anything from that does not happen to him, the matter is lighter.
And the cure of him that happens from that is, as you know, a sedative wax: so that you put it on a broken spine with rose oil only: or when you wipe it off.
Place over it the thing a few times in the day until he sits down when he is sedated: then place over one of the places that comforts, dries, and ties to him with a ligament: and order the sick person to use quietly and tranquilly. And do not let him sleep on the side where he feels pain until he is healed.
If it happens that there is a rupture of the bone joint, or something that has already been separated from it: then you should, as it were, separate the wound on the skin and remove it. Then gather together the two sides of the wound with a stitch. Then take care of himself with some ointment, from the unguent, until he is healed.
If, however, the bone is finally broken at the end of the shinbone, and the end is severed: then you should, with the thumb of the left hand, introduce it into the socket of the bone that has been broken, and with the right hand, as well as you can, and by means of a second equating.
Then put it back on itself, and take it off: and take care of the wound with the same cure that preceded it, until it is healed.
The Restoration of Bones.
CHAPTERXI.
Bones of Ankhs rarely break. If they do break, then their fracture is not, unless the break is at the ends of them: and they split along their length, and bend inward: and there results a pain and a bruise in the joint where it breaks: and the leg is lame because it is broken.
And its restoration is such that you should stretch out your hand over it, until you stand on the fracture as in this figure (show).
If the fracture is only at the ends of it, then make it equal, according to the way you are prepared by means of an equating, until it resembles its natural shape.
But if the fracture is along its length, or has already bent inward: then let the sick man lie on his stomach, until such time as its restoration is prepared for you. When you have made it equal, then place over it an emprastum: then place over it a wooden frame, or a strip of skin: and string with a tight bond so that you do not change the fracture, and do not remove the frame.

On the Restoration of a Broken Arm.
The arm is composed of two bones, which are called two fascicles.
Of which one is small; and that is what follows the thumb. And the other is large, and that is what is placed under the little finger on the lower side.
Perhaps therefore the larger fascicle breaks alone, or the smaller one: and perhaps they both break at once.
But when the smaller fascicle breaks above, then the restoration of it is easy, and its cure is easier.
And when the larger one breaks below, this is a bad break, and its cure is difficult.
And it is worse when two bones break at the same time.
But if the bone that is broken is only a little above, then it is suitable for the doctor to restore it with ease until it is equal to the other.
But if the broken bone is a large one, then he should put on a stronger bandage tightly.
And he should put on the shape of the hand at the restoration of it and a long extension over the little finger. Then let him extend the arm from the lower part, or with his hand, or with a bandage. And another minister should extend it from above.
Then the doctor should make the bone equal, until he reduces it to the best shape he can get.
If there are any fractures in the bones, then you should strive to reduce any fragment in its place with your power.
But if it seems to you that there is a separation in it, and it stings the skin, and you have no hope of restoring it: then find on top of it, and take it off according to the manner which we have said in those which precede.
But if the fracture is with the wrist, I have already made to this single capiter, so you take the cure of it from there.

And let there be cloths wrapped around the fracture, soft, wet cloths: and let them not be hard, rough. And let there be a cord that is twisted from linen itself, middle between thin and thick, as we have described it.
And consider the member and ligaments after days.
