On the subject of dislocation.
Dislocation is the exit of a joint from its place. Why does it restrain itself from its own movement, and is fixed in form. And causes great pain to the weak and painful injuries.
When therefore a dislocation happens, then let him hurry to restore it: he should not delay it in any way.
For if he is delayed, the place will become stiff, and with it may come a reduction of the dislocation.
Therefore, it should not be moved, nor be extended in the time of its stiffening. For it often causes a spasm to occur, and painful injuries.
But when that happens, then let him prepare for the bloodletting. Then dismiss it, until it quiets down with the stiffness.
Then the member is kneaded with warm water and oil. Then reduce it with ease.
And heal all members with it, when the memory of the remedy comes to its place.
I have already arranged the chapters on dislocations and also on fractures, from the upper part of the body to the lower.
On the treatment of a dislocated jaw (lower).
The two jaws are rarely dislocated.
And the dislocation of the jaw occurs in only one of two ways. Or they are removed from their places by a small distance, and are slightly softened. Or they are dislocated completely and intact, until they are softened towards the breast, until the saliva of the wound runs away, and he is not able to keep it shut, nor can he close his mouth, and his tongue hangs out with a gurgling sound.
When the dislocation of this is small, then it returns to its place by itself with a little difficulty.
If the dislocation is complete and intact: then it should be administered to reduce it quickly, and there should be no delay: and it is as if the doctor holds the jaw with his thumb at the base of the dislocation between his teeth, if the dislocation is from one side only. Or introduce two thumbs simultaneously, if the dislocation is from both sides. And the remaining fingers of his hand are outside, with which he equalizes it.
And order the sick person to clean his jaws, and allow them to chew on all sides. And the doctor equalizes the jaw and pushes it back into place.
If reducing it is difficult, especially if both jaws are involved: then administer a vapour with hot water and oil until the reduction becomes easy: do not delay reducing the jaws at all, as we have said.
When they are closed and equalized, and the mouth is shut, and they do not soften: then place a few cotton pads over them, with a patch made of wax: and rub it with rose oil.
Then bind it with ease using soft ligaments.
Then let him sleep with ease on his back; and let his head rest between two pillows so that he does not move to the right or left: nor let him chew anything; but let his food be soft, until such time as the pains ease, and let the jaws be loosed: then let him eat what he wants, and give it to him easily.
Do not cause pain during the surgery on his mouth or when drinking or swallowing, until such time as the jaws are loosed, and he is healed.
If it is difficult to reduce the two jaws when they are dislocated at some time and do not return to their places: then often fever and sodium result from this; and perhaps the stomach becomes weak, and perhaps pure choler is discharged.
So when you see this: then know that it is a loss, and he often dies who experiences this in ten days.
Regarding the reduction of a dislocated femur and shoulder blade.
The furcata (shoulder blade) does not dislocate from the inside due to its connection with the chest. And it dislocations when it is pushed outward: and gives a sense of pain.
And its restoration is so that he lies sick on his back, and extends his arms. Then, with a strong grasp of your hand, bind the place, for it will return to its place.
Then place a cloth on top, and hairs, and tighten it.
The extremities that follow the bed, and continue with him, do not dislocate unless they are rarely pushed. If it dislocations on a certain day: then it should be reduced and made even as we have said, and you should be prepared.
Then place a cloth on top, and tighten it. He should instruct the sick person to adhere to rest and tranquility until they recover.
And with this same cure, when the extremity of the humerus is reduced, it should also be separated from its place.
De reductione dislocationis Humeri.
You should know that the humerus does not dislocate except by three means.
Of which one is such that it dislocates to the tickling side toward the lower part. Of which second is such that it dislocates to the part of the chest. And perhaps it dislocates to the upper part of the humerus: and this happens rarely.
And does not dislocate toward the back, because of the spatula. And does not dislocate toward the front, because of the nerves.
And indeed it most frequently dislocates and exits to the lower side toward the tickling: mainly in those who have thin flesh. Since it exits them quickly, and enters them quickly.
But in those whom the flesh is abundant, it is the opposite; that is, because it exits them with difficulty, and enters them with difficulty.
And perhaps in some people there is a blow, or a fall, and the humerus is dislocated from the warm; and it is thought because of that, since it has already been dislocated.
It should therefore be such that you experience this, until you are certain about its state, and then proceed with its treatment.
And the dislocation is recognized when it is toward the lower side toward the tickling: and you distinguish between a dislocated humerus and a healthy one. For there is a great difference between them; and in the head of the humerus there is a concavity, and under the tickling at the touch of the head of the humerus it seems to be the same as itself: and the sick person cannot bring his hand to his ear, nor move it in all its movements.
And likewise, if dislocated towards the breast, or upward: then the injury is evident to the touch, and is not hidden.
And of this dislocation it is easy to reduce when it is recent, or when the boy is weak.
And its reduction is, that he should lift his own hand towards the top. Then put your fingers under his nipple, and with force raise the joint up to its place: and the attendant should lift his hand, and extend it upward. Then let him put it down, for himself he will quickly return.
But if he does not return by that which we have said, and there are many days from the time of the dislocation: then it should be, that the sick person bathes in warm water, and an emollient is administered which softens and soothes, such as if roots of aloe, and foenugraecum, and mellilotum were boiled in water, and administered.
Then the sick person should lie on his back: and let there be placed under his nipple a sphere and wool, which is equal to the tenderness and the constriction.
Then the doctor should place his heel over the sphere, and with force should draw the shoulder-blade downward, and should lift the attendant's hand towards the bottom.
And if you wish, reduce it according to this method: and it is so, that the present male who is longer should stand by the side, and introduce his shoulder under the nipple of the attendant: and lift the nipple of him upward, until the sick person hangs suspended in the air. Then another attendant should hold the attendant' hand downward. But if the sick person is light: then it should be, that he be suspended with something else, so that he be heavy: for the dislocation will immediately return.
It is also reduced in another way: and it is so arranged that the wood of a tree which is long and has a round head, like the handle of a mortar, should not be thick or fine. Then put a thin cloth under the body after they have placed it on top of the wood's head: and the sick man should stand according to the length of the wood.
Then his hands are extended to the lower part from one side, and his body is extended from the other side with strength: for the junction returns to its place with velocity.
If his reduction is difficult through all that we have said, then use this cure: and it is so that you take a piece of wood the length of two arms, and its width is the width of four fingers, and its thickness is the thickness of two fingers; and let its head be round, so that it may relieve the entrance to the deep pit, according to this form.
Then thin cloth should be tied over the round head so that it does not hurt the sick wood.
Then the sick person should be placed under the pit so that his hands or arm can be extended over the wood to the lower part; and the wood should be tied to the aid and arm and end of the hand.
Then an arm should be placed on the step by its width, and the hand should be extended to the lower part, and the remaining body should be suspended from one side. The junction enters immediately.
When the entrance has thus been completed in any way possible, then you should place under the pit a sphere of equal size made of wool. Then place a prepared plug made of ground incense with olive oil and white powder on the whole shoulder, as it rises from above.
Then tie the string under the pit with a tight knot: and let it rise above the plug; and connect his hands to his neck, and leave them alone for seven days.
It is necessary that food be given to the sick person until he feels better, because it is faster for him to heal.
Then after seven or five days he should be given more, and then he should be examined with movement. If then it is firm and does not soften, then it is well healed.
If then the joint is dislocated many times, because of the moisture that occurs with it, or for another reason: then it is necessary that cauterization be administered to it with three holes, according to what has been said in the chapter on cauterization.
That if this is done entirely, and the ligament is loosened after seven days, and the joint does not firm up, and the splint and tightness on it are sometimes removed, and it does not harden, and falls, and cannot raise itself to the top: then you should know that the nerves that go to the shoulder have already been cut, or stretched out, or softened: and you should know that the joint is never fixed in its place.
But dislocation that happens toward the chest and nipple, or backward reduction, is made with an impact and extension with the hands: until it returns, and cauterization is administered to it with remaining tightness and care, until it heals.
That if something happens after healing with a heavy weight on the member, and delay in its movement: then the sick person should use baths many times, until that heaviness is softened, and return to its own nature first.
On the treatment of dislocation of the cubitus bone.
You should know that the joint of the cubitus dislocates with difficulty, and in the same way it returns with difficulty, too: and it itself dislocates to all parts, especially to the front, or to the back.
And its dislocation does not conceal itself from you: because it falls with a noise, and with a touch, according to whatever figure it dislocates. And when you join the broken hip with a sound one, let it be manifest to thee. For the joint will be broken, and the arm cannot be doubled, nor can it touch its own shoulder.
And you ought to reduce the dislocation immediately, before a cold apostema comes upon him.
For if a hot apostema comes upon him, it is difficult to reduce; and he may not be cured at all, especially when it is behind. For this is worse in every respect than dislocation, and more painful; and often death comes with it.
And the restoration of it is when it can be reduced, to extend his hand with two hands; and his arm is spread out, and the doctor's fingers are above and below the hip, and he pushes the dislocation with his thumbs of his hands simultaneously, or with the root of his palm, until he returns to his place.
Or rather the dislocation is to the front; then he returns in such a way that he doubles his hand with his own hand until he hits against the root of his palm the hip that is dislocated.
Which if he does not obey the dislocation to reduction, then administer a harsh and sharp extension; and it is as if two ministers extend the arm: and they hold him too, so that he does not move during the extension.
Then the arm is rotated to all parts after it has been turned over a blanket of cloth doubled long, or a wide ligament.
And when the doctor touches the joint with his own hand, he should anoint it with oil so that the joint moves easily with lubrication. Then he pushes the dislocation with a harsh push until it is reduced.
And after its reduction, let a poultice be placed on it, in which there is stinging, and drying with chalk powder; and it is bound tightly going down. And hang the arm from the neck of the nurse: and let it rest for several days.
Then untie it. But if the joint has set in place, then loosen the ligature from it, and let it be.
And if you see that the joint is not well tied, then renew the plaster and the ligature, and let it be: for several days let it rest, until it is tied. Then untie it.
But if the weight comes to him after the joint has been reduced and he is in motion: then administer humidification in balneation, and subtle friction, and pressure, until it has healed, or put something over the joint, that is, nat-o-me-um (a mix of olive oil and vinegar). Then tie it up, and let it be: for a day and a night. Then take it off, and put him in the bath. But when he sweats: then tie the joint with subtle friction. Then repeat over the joint with a single stroke, and another, and a third time with the entrance into the bath, until it has softened.
And if you want: put over the joint dung, moist, heated with butter, and tie over it.
Do this a few times. This softens it, and it returns to its position.
The cure for dislocation by Albucasis.
Mahessem dislocates the hand, that is, the joint which is between the arm and the hand.
And the reduction of his dislocation is easy. But the other joints are difficult to reduce. However, it is necessary that speed be there in the reduction of his dislocation at the time when he dislocates, before the measurement is taken: or happens to him in the case of hot weather.
And the reduction of his dislocation is this: that you put Mahessem's weak hand on a table, and let him stretch out his finger of the hand: and that the doctor puts his palm on the eminence of the joint, and pushes it, until it returns.
Indeed, it should be considered if the palm has already receded to the abdomen of the hand: then place the sick hand of his behind a table at extension and reduction; and if dislocation is going towards the back of the hand, then the hand's position should be intrinsece on the table, that is, the inner part of the hand should be placed over the table so that the doctor's hand falls over this eminence of the joint.
If it has returned immediately, then it is good; and if not, then bind it with a plaster while it sits with an apostema: and let him go, and do not return to it.
For if he does not tolerate it, and you cannot bring it to reduction after the days have passed, yet the joint remains according to its tortuosity: and there is no harm in an infirm man unless his hand becomes soft, and he cannot clench anything: then you should know that the nerves are cut off or rubbed.
Therefore, it is not a talent in this, except to bind it with a suture near its danger. And perhaps it does not yield anything.
So when he does not return to Al-Mahasım, then place on him a plaster which we have described. Then bind it and let it go for five days: then untie it and let it go, and exercise the hand.
But if its movement is prevented, and something heavy happens to it: then soften it with hot water and sometimes with friction, until it softens.
The Treatment of Dislocation of the Digits
When a finger is dislocated from all sides, or from the back of the palm, or from its bottom: then extend the finger, and push the dislocation with your thumb until it returns.
Then support the tip of the finger, and suspend it towards the side to which it is dislocated, and let him go for two days.
Then suspend him and extend him until he is even with the straight line on that day.
And then, when it is night, bind him according to his narrative: and do not cease to unbind him on that day: and exercise him with motion: and bind him at night.
Make it for a few days until he is tied up.
And also do the same with him if his shoulder comes out of place: and bind him to the side that is hurt, and do your work with him until he heals.
And also do the same with him when his shoulder or any other part comes out of place.
§ On the treatment of dislocations of the spine (back).
When it happens to anyone that the spine or the neck is completely out of joint, or that many spines have been removed from their places: there is no cure for this disease, for death approaches the sick person.
And its sign is that the sick person leaves off working willingly, and cannot hold anything: and often some of his limbs become weak from him, or two of his limbs, or two of his arms, or one of them: or one of his spines is removed from its place.
For often one is removed again, since it often happens that they are moved on all four sides.
That which is moved to the back is called  (curved) Hump.
But the cure for that is so that if the   (curved) Hump has happened from infancy: there is no cure for it, and there is no healing at all from it.
But in that which has happened from a fall, or from being struck, or from similar things: the old people have long ago told you about the ways of curing it. For most of them do not return with profit.
I have however shortened that from him, for it does not excuse his being far away from my intention: and my exposure of it and forming an instrument with many different ways out of it, and showing it to himself.
I say then, that in a deformity which occurs from the front part, there is no talent, nor healing from it.
And likewise in that which was made on both sides.
And not to be cured from them, unless that which happens in the back of its own is considered: and it is like this, that the sick person extends over his face on a platform equal near the wall: and he expands under it a damp cloth, so that his chest is not hurt.
Then a straight piece of wood should be placed in the footrest on the ground, at the end of the leg, about the head of it: and another piece of wood should be held around the person's feet at the other end of the leg: and the assistant should hold the wood steady, so that it does not stand firm in the footrest. And the other assistant should hold the other one.
Then he will turn his head with a soft cloth, and fasten it firmly: and he will extend the end of the ligament to the wood that is at the head: and he will tie himself up in it.
Then he will tighten another ligament above his shoulders, and above his knees, and around his loins. Then he will tie all the ligaments together, and tie them to the other wood that is at his feet.
Then the assistant will stretch all the ligaments to the side of the wood, and do not remove the two woods from their places where they are fixed: however, they should not be fixed, as we have said.
And the doctor should place his palms on the spine with strength, until he has straightened out: or he should place them on the board. Then he will attach his feet to the board, until he has straightened out.
If, however, he does not recover with this treatment, then take a board whose length is about three arms. And the wall where we have spoken of, which is near the sick person, should be a place into which the end of the board can be inserted. Then put the middle piece of the board over the spine. And let the doctor put his feet on the other end. And tighten it well until the spine is cinched and return to its place.
Then if you want to operate with a tourniquet that is held by your hand: and it is so, that you set on the ground at the head of the bed two logs whose length sits of an arm: and at the head of those two logs.
And the length that is between all the wood is one palm: and now it is done at every place a hole, where the tourniquet runs: and all the wood is made firm on the ground, so that it does not move at all.
And the round wood is inserted: and the tourniquet is in two holes of the two woods.
And at the end of it is a hole, in which the wood is fastened, whose length is a palm with that which rotates: and in two other woods there is the same.
Then tighten the ligatures that you have tightened in the chest of the patient with the tourniquet that is at his head: and the ligatures that you have tightened in his thighs with the tourniquet that is at his feet.
Then stand next to every tourniquet assistant who twists his hand with the key, with which the tourniquet rotates: and the doctor makes even the spine, according to what we said.
This is the form of the tourniquet, which follows.
Then after the spine has returned and evened out, then it is necessary that you put on dried blood with albumen. Then a pillow. Then put above the blood-soaked cloth a board whose width is three fingers, or about them. And the length of it what takes up the space of the hollowness, and on the side of the spine healthy.
Then tighten with a ligature, which we have said. The sick should be fed with a subtle diet until they are well.
If there remains any sign of a malady at the end of recovery: then it is necessary that the medicines which soften and relieve be given with a bland diet over an extended period of time. And it is given to him in that leaden bed.
And when there happens a malady at the very end of the back spine: and it is thought that by this dislocation, and it is this mouth which now emerges. Why should he not be put to death with such medicine: for it might perhaps cause death.
On the treatment of a dislocated ankle.
You should know that a dislocated ankle, and a dislocated shoulder, do not occur unless there is a complete dislocation: nor does it occur to them what happens with other joints due to a small distance and pressure.
And the joint of the ankle dislocates according to four parts. Because it dislocates towards the front and towards the back.
And indeed it mostly dislocates towards the inside: and little towards the front and back.
And the treatment for this dislocation to the inside is: because when you join the healthy thigh with the sick one, it is longer: and his knee joint emerges more than that of a healthy person, and the sick person cannot double his leg at the hip: and there is a warm place following the hip, so that the head of the hip has already reached it.
And the sign of him who has suffered a dislocation to the outside is, that are opposable signs to these signs.
And the sign of him who has suffered a dislocation to the front is, that he spreads out the healthy thigh according to its complement: however, he does not double it without pain in his leg: and if he tries to walk, he cannot step forward on to the front: and he holds back his urine, and is thrust out at the hip, and during walking he catches his foot on the heel.
And the sign of him who has dislocated his hip behind is that he cannot spread his knees without spreading his hips first, and his leg is shorter than the other, and his hips are softened, and the head of the femur is at the place of the hip joint.
The reduction of this dislocation is that you should consider then, if the dislocation is fresh, which now grows old with one who has it. Then, if you administer the reduction of it, and remains after its own disposition: then there is no cure at all in it. Therefore it should not be assumed that he is completely cured.
But in that one whose dislocation is fresh, and is of the four kinds of dislocations: then approach to this, so that you may loose it, and extend it towards the inside and outside, and move it with your right and left hand: for perhaps it will recover itself, and will not need any other cure.
But if this does not happen in this way, then you should prepare a strong instrument, and extend his leg from the lower part: or with its hands, or with a ligature that binds above the leg: and another minister should extend his hand from above the knee, so that he can put his hands under the skin. Then he should tie with a soft ligature on the root of the hip, and hold the end of the other ligament.
And let his extension be either from the front part of the groin to the side of the thigh, or from the back to the side of the spine.
And let all these extensions be bent at once, until he raises himself up with his body from the ground, and remains hanging.
This is indeed a common type of reduction for all four kinds of dislocations.
But if the dislocation returns with this that I have said, it is good: and if not, then it is necessary for all kinds to say what I mean about their own cure.
For in his own reduction when dislocation is to the interiors, it should be so that the sick person lies on the healthy side of him. Then he places the ligament over the root of the pelvis, in that which is between the head of the pelvis, and the place which is under the groin. Then he extends the ligament to the top from the part of the groin to the higher side of the body, to the part of the fence.
Then another strong minister takes his arm, and diminishes the place of the large pelvis of the sick person, and extends it to the externals with a great extension. For he has already returned to his place.
And this species is easier than the other species of cure, by which this member is reduced.
If, however, he does not comply with you at all in this entry with this species of cure: then it should be so that two nurses are bound together with a strong ligament, soft, over the two heels, and over the two knees: and let the length of each from its comparison be the quantity of four fingers: and let the trunk of the sick person be extended by one more quantity of two fingers.
Then the sick person should be suspended on the head of the wood, which is in the house: and let the length of him from the ground be the quantity of two arms.
Then command a strong young man to support the head of the pelvis: and let another suspend the sick one with his back: and let another push him with strength, for the turning returns to its place with speed.
But in his own reduction, when there is a dislocation to the externals, it should be so that the sick person lies on the ground, according to which we have spoken in having gibbosity. And let a ligament be tied around his own weak pelvis and over his chest.
Then two logs are placed, one at the feet of him, and another at the head of him. Then a log is placed in the middle of the pelvis, fixed securely: over which moist cloths are spread so as not to wound the sick person: and let the wood be between his hips, so that it does not attract towards the lower side by extension.
Then all the attendants should spread out to their respective parts and the doctor should give a careful reduction with his hand.
If he therefore submits to reversion, it will be good.
And if not, place her on the table, and press her equally as we said in the case of a hunchback. However, it is necessary that the sick person lie on the sound side.
And when dislocation is to the front, then it is necessary that the leg of the attendant be spread quickly out; and it is according to this story on a platform: and the doctor should place his hand with his right hand on the knee of the sick person. Then he should tie him up with the other hand, and with that tying place him stretched out to the lower side of the knee.
And if dislocation is to the back: then it is not necessary that the sick person be stretched out to the lower side, and let him sit upright on the ground; but he should be placed on a hard surface, as it is also if his ankle dislocates to the outside, according to what we said about his lying on two ankles: and it is on his face, and the ligaments are extended; according to what we have just said.
And it is necessary that compression with the table be administered also to the place from which the injury comes out, when reduction is completed in accordance with what is necessary.
And the sign of its return should not be hidden from you: and it is so that you spread out the two legs of the attendants; and when you see them equal, and the sick person tightens his leg, and stretches himself out without complaint: then you will know that his member has returned, in accordance with what is necessary; then join together the two hips, and place a splint, and tie it with a cord, so that it does not move to any part: and let the sick person be quiet for three or four days.
Then loosen the ligaments, and splint, and bring together the legs with each other; then if you see them equal in position, then you will know that the dislocation is now healed; so release the sick person to walk about.
And if you see any softening: then return, and patch it up, and tighten it according to your first order: and also release it after three days.
Then remove it and make the wound heal well on its own for a few days, until it is healed.
[On the Dislocation of the Knee.]
[The knee may be dislocated] in three ways: first, by straightening the leg; second, by flex (It may be dislocated) to the outside, to the inside, and to the bottom, that is, to the back.
And it is not dislocated to the front.
[And the sign of its dislocation is that he who has the injury should apply his knee to his hip.] But if it does not attach to the hip: then you shall know that the knee is dislocated.
[And the restoration of all types of dislocation is so that the sick person may sit upright and so that his leg may extend behind it if he can, and fasten his back to his face: and place his foot between your feet.
Then attach the knee of the injured person with your palms, and hold them straight between your fingers over his knee. Then apply your two palms to the sides of his knee with strength. And another person extends his foot, until he brings the knee back to its place.
[And the sign of restoration is that the hip should attach to the knee with ease, not with effort.]
Then patch him up, and fasten the hip to the knee. Then bind them together at the same time with sutures for three or four days. Then remove the bindings.
And do not let the sick person walk for a few days until he is healed.
But if you do not bring him back to you by a strong extension of the ligaments, which is described in the cure of aching joints, until he returns to his place: then bind him up with ligatures, and administer a strong extension with the ligaments, until he heals.
On the treatment of a dislocated calcaneus.
When the calcaneus is occasionally displaced with only a small degree, and sometimes dislocated from its second component. And this displacement of it occurs either internally or externally.
And the sign of this displacement is that you see the calcaneus swollen, protruding toward the side to which it is displaced.
The cure for this displacement is easy; and it is to extend it with ease by the hands, and to make it even, until it comes back.
But in the treatment of it when it is dislocated from its second component, you should make the sick person sit upright: and hold him by the rear part of his back, firmly in the middle of him.
Then take hold of it with your right hand from the upper part of it, and with your left hand from the lower part of the foot near the nail.
Then draw the foot toward you with your right hand. Then with two fingers of your left hand.
Then extend it with your left hand, and press the chest of the foot toward the leg with your right hand without any effort.
Do this twice, as we have described.
Then press the chest of the foot toward the leg in a third direction, and you pull on the nail.
If it then comes back in a single direction or in two directions like this, and you see the foot even, it is good; but if not, then repeat the action on him: for he will come back.
But if it is forbidden to you to reduce it with that which we have described: then let the sick person lie on his back on the ground, and fix a sturdy leg in the ground so that it falls between his thighs; and you may now roll over him the cloth so as not to hurt him.
Then let the servant of the sick person take his leg: then the other servant shall extend it, or with his hands, or with a band that binds around the neck of the foot. Then all the ministers shall stand against the extension of their own body; and the nurse shall stand between the two hips holding him, so that his body is not drawn down to the lower side during the extension.
Then the doctor shall make his hands equal, and the other minister shall hold the healthy leg from below. For the dislocation returns with velocity.
When therefore the dislocation returns, and appears to you that the restoration is sound: then place a strap and support, and tighten it with ligaments firmly, and bind the foot with the ligament to the leg.
And it is necessary that you should beware and fear the nerves which are above the heel at the back, lest the ligaments be too tight on them, and they hurt them.
Then let him go for two days, or three. That if the ligament softens, tighten it again: then حلّه in a third day, or a fourth day: and forbid the sick from walking for forty days. But if he should attempt to walk before that space, there is no security but the dislocation would break again, and be corrupted, and receive no cure after it.
That if he should happen to have a feverish swelling: then you shall administer to him in a sedative that which we have already mentioned in another place from the treatment, and an emulsion, until it subsides.
Of the dislocation of the toes of the foot and their cure.
It is necessary that he should be equal when he is dislocated from them with a little extension without work: and that is not difficult, indeed, it is easy.
But if the dislocation is in some of the joints of the back of the foot: then it is necessary that he should make his sick foot stand on an equal place of the earth, or on a table: and it stands as one who walks.
Then stand still, and put your foot upon that which protrudes from them by the joint. Then press that bone with your foot with strength, until it returns, and the appearance is already equal, and does not appear at all in the place eminence.
Then put under the stomach of his foot a plate which catches the whole foot, which has two heads. Then tighten him with a strap for three days. Then solve him, and let him rest from walking for several days, until he is tightened and made secure by the reduction.
As for the species of dislocation with the ulna or fracture.
When something like this happens and you are concerned about his cure and recovery, often death succeeds: and because of this you should not presumptiously take upon yourself to direct his cure, which is of this kind, unless you be prudent in the art, compassionate, merciful, slow, not bold, and not presumptuous; and use only such medicines as sit on the aphorism's warm sides: and dismiss the sick with divine judgment. Unless you hope for salvation from perdition with gentleness of sickness, and in which there appears some hope of salvation. Consider therefore the reduction of him at once at the beginning of the thing, before a warm aphorism happens.
But if the member returns, according to our directions, then administer a regime which sits on the aphorism; and care for the ulna with him when it suits him from an ointment of dried herbs.
That if the dislocation is with fracture, and things in the bones separate, then consider removal of them, and do in him what we said in simple illnesses because it preceded in those places.
And be careful according to your strength, and guard yourself from the entrance into a path of fear: because my prediction went before you.
For that is better for you with your Creator, and more lasting to your glory, and more safe to your body with them in your time.
